1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# big-code-pg-arch-001: PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline

## Task

Trace the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline from parse to execute. Starting from `exec_simple_query()` in the traffic cop, map how a SQL string flows through the parser (lexer + grammar), semantic analyzer, query rewriter, planner/optimizer, and executor, identifying the data structures that flow between each stage.

## Context

- **Repository**: github.com/sg-evals/postgres--5a461dc4 (mirror of github.com/sg-evals)/postgres--5a461dc4 (mirror of postgres/postgres) (C, ~1.5M LOC)
- **Category**: Architectural Understanding
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: src/backend/ — parser, rewrite, optimizer, executor subsystems

## Requirements

1. Identify all files involved in the query pipeline (traffic cop, parser, analyzer, rewriter, planner, executor, node type definitions)
2. Trace the dependency chain from `exec_simple_query()` through each pipeline stage, identifying the data type transformations (RawStmt -> Query -> PlannedStmt -> tuples)
3. Document the two-phase optimization (Path generation via allpaths.c, then Plan creation via createplan.c)
4. Explain the Volcano-style executor dispatch in execProcnode.c

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — role in architecture
- path/to/file2.ext — role in architecture
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Entry point: path/to/entry.ext
2. Calls: path/to/next.ext (via function/method name)
3. Delegates to: path/to/impl.ext
...

## Analysis
[Detailed architectural analysis including:
- Design patterns identified
- Component responsibilities
- Data flow description
- Interface contracts between components]

## Summary
[Concise 2-3 sentence summary answering the task question]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- File recall: Did you find the correct set of architecturally relevant files?
- Dependency accuracy: Did you trace the correct dependency/call chain?
- Architectural coherence: Did you correctly identify the design patterns and component relationships?

435 /logs/agent/solution.md
   - **Pull-based semantics**: Child nodes called to fetch input, not pushed by parents.
   - Returns a `TupleTableSlot` wrapping the next result tuple (or NULL if exhausted).
   - **Node-specific logic**:
     - **Scan nodes** (SeqScan, IndexScan): Fetch from storage layer, apply filters.
     - **Join nodes** (HashJoin, NestLoop, MergeJoin): Combine tuples from left/right children.
     - **Aggregate nodes** (Agg, GroupAggregate): Accumulate state, emit group results.
     - **Sort nodes** (Sort, IncrementalSort): Buffer input, sort in-memory or on disk, return sorted stream.
     - **Limit nodes** (Limit): Count output tuples, stop after N.

3. **Finalization Phase** (`ExecEndNode`)
   - Cleans up node-specific state.
   - Closes relations and indexes.
   - Frees memory.
   - Recursively finalizes child nodes.

**Dispatcher mechanism** (execProcnode.c):
- Each `PlanState` holds a function pointer to its executor function.
- `ExecProcNode()` calls this pointer, dispatching to the correct implementation.
- Enables polymorphic node execution without explicit type checks.

**Key files**:
- `src/backend/executor/execProcnode.c` — Dispatcher framework and state initialization
- `src/backend/executor/execMain.c` — Main ExecutorStart/Run/Finish/End loop
- `src/backend/executor/node*.c` — 60+ individual node implementations

### 7. Data Structures & Type Hierarchy

**Core node types used in the pipeline**:
- **RawStmt** (parsenodes.h): Parser output; minimal semantics, unvalidated names/types.
- **Query** (parsenodes.h): Analyzer output; full semantics, catalog-resolved names, type-checked expressions.
- **RelOptInfo / Path** (pathnodes.h): Optimizer internal; represents candidate access plans with cost estimates.
- **Plan / PlanState** (plannodes.

## Summary of Analysis

The solution documents:

### ✅ **Files Identified** (35+ files)
- **Traffic cop**: `src/backend/tcop/postgres.c` - Entry point
- **Parser**: scan.l, gram.y, parser.c - Lexical/syntactic analysis
- **Analyzer**: analyze.c, parse_*.c - Semantic analysis
- **Rewriter**: rewriteHandler.c - Rule/view rewriting
- **Optimizer**: planner.c, planmain.c, allpaths.c, createplan.c - Two-phase optimization
- **Executor**: execMain.c, execProcnode.c, node*.c - Volcano-style execution

### ✅ **Complete Dependency Chain**
Traced from `exec_simple_query()` through all 6 pipeline stages:
1. **Parsing** → RawStmt (syntactic AST)
2. **Semantic Analysis** → Query (normalized logical tree)
3. **Rewriting** → Query (rule-rewritten)
4. **Path Generation** → Path candidates with costs
5. **Plan Creation** → PlannedStmt (executable plan)
6. **Execution** → Tuple stream (results)

### ✅ **Two-Phase Optimization Documented**
- **Phase 1** (allpaths.c): Enumerate all possible access paths (join orders, scan methods) with cost estimates
- **Phase 2** (createplan.c): Select lowest-cost path and convert to executable Plan tree

### ✅ **Volcano Executor Architecture Explained**
- `ExecInitNode()` - Recursively initialize PlanState tree
- `ExecProcNode()` - Dispatch to node-specific executors via function pointers
- `ExecEndNode()` - Recursively finalize nodes
- Pull-based tuple-at-a-time evaluation with 60+ node type implementations

The slots returned by ExecSeqScan() may contain\n60:  *\t\ttuples which contain the attributes ExecNestLoop() uses to\n61:  *\t\tform the tuples it returns.\n62:  *\n63:  *\t  * Eventually ExecSeqScan() stops returning tuples and the nest\n64:  *\t\tloop join ends.   "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: src/backend/optimizer/README\n2: \n3: Optimizer\n4: =========\n5: \n6: These directories take the Query structure returned by the parser, and\n7: generate a plan used by the executor.  The /plan directory generates the\n8: actual output plan, the /path code generates all possible ways to join the\n9: tables, and /prep handles various preprocessing steps for special cases.\n10: /util is utility stuff.  /geqo is the separate \"genetic optimization\" planner\n11: --- it does a semi-random search through the join tree space, rather than\n12: exhaustively considering all possible join trees.  (But each join considered\n13: by /geqo is given to /path to create paths for, so we consider all possible\n14: implementation paths for each specific join pair even in GEQO mode.)\n15: \n16: \n17: Paths and Join Pairs\n18: --------------------\n19: \n20: During the planning/optimizing process, we build \"Path\" trees representing\n21: the different ways of doing a query.  We select the cheapest Path that\n22: generates the desired relation and turn it into a Plan to pass to the\n23: executor.  (There is pretty nearly a one-to-one correspondence between the\n24: Path and Plan trees, but Path nodes omit info that won't be needed during\n25: planning, and include info needed for planning that won't be needed by the\n26: executor.)\n27: \n28: The optimizer builds a RelOptInfo structure for each base relation used in\n29: the query.  Base rels are either primitive tables, or subquery subselects\n30: that are planned via a separate recursive invocation of the planner.  A\n31: RelOptInfo is also built for each join relation that is considered during\n32: planning.  A join rel is simply a combination of base rels.  There is only\n33: one join RelOptInfo for any given set of baserels --- for example, the join\n34: {A B C} is represented by the same RelOptInfo no matter whether we build it\n35: by joining A and B first and then adding C, or joining B and C first and\n36: then adding A, etc.  These different means of building the joinrel are\n37: represented as Paths.  For each RelOptInfo we build a list of Paths that\n38: represent plausible ways to implement the scan or join of that relation.\n39: Once we've considered all the plausible Paths for a rel, we select the one\n40: that is cheapest according to the planner's cost estimates.  The final plan\n41: is derived from the cheapest Path for the RelOptInfo that includes all the\n42: base rels of the query.\n43: \n44: Possible Paths for a primitive table relation include plain old sequential\n45: scan, plus index scans for any indexes that exist on the table, plus bitmap\n46: index scans using one or more indexes.  Specialized RTE types, such as\n47: function RTEs, may have only one possible Path.\n48: \n49: Joins always occur using two RelOptInfos.  One is outer, the other inner.\n50: Outers drive lookups of values in the inner.  In a nested loop, lookups of\n51: values in the inner occur by scanning the inner path once per outer tuple\n52: to find each matching inner row.  In a mergejoin, inner and outer rows are\n53: ordered, and are accessed in order, so only one scan is required to perform\n54: the entire join: both inner and outer paths are scanned in-sync.  (There's\n55: not a lot of difference between inner and outer in a mergejoin...)  In a\n56: hashjoin, the inner is scanned first and all its rows are entered in a\n57: hashtable, then the outer is scanned and for each row we lookup the join\n58: key in the hashtable.\n59: \n60: A Path for a join relation is actually a tree structure, with the topmost\n61: Path node representing the last-applied join method.  It has left and right\n62: subpaths that represent the scan or join methods used for the two input\n63: relations.\n64: \n65: \n66: Join Tree Construction\n67: ----------------------\n68: \n69: The optimizer generates optimal query plans by doing a more-or-less\n70: exhaustive search through the ways of executing the query.  The best Path\n71: tree is found by a recursive process:\n72: \n73: 1) Take each base relation in the query, and make a RelOptInfo structure\n74: for it.  Find each potentially useful way of accessing the relation,\n75: including sequential and index scans, and make Paths representing those\n76: ways.  All the Paths made for a given relation are placed in its\n77: RelOptInfo.pathlist.  (Actually, we discard Paths that are obviously\n78: inferior alternatives before they ever get into the pathlist --- what\n79: ends up in the pathlist is the cheapest way of generating each potentially\n80: useful sort ordering and parameterization of the relation.)  Also create a\n81: RelOptInfo.joininfo list including all the join clauses that involve this\n82: relation.  For example, the WHERE clause \"tab1.col1 = tab2.col1\" generates\n83: entries in both tab1 and tab2's joininfo lists.\n84: \n85: If we have only a single base relation in the query, we are done.\n86: Otherwise we have to figure out how to join the base relations into a\n87: single join relation.\n88: \n89: 2) Normally, any explicit JOIN clauses are \"flattened\" so that we just\n90: have a list of relations to join.  However, FULL OUTER JOIN clauses are\n91: never flattened, and other kinds of JOIN might not be either, if the\n92: flattening process is stopped by join_collapse_limit or from_collapse_limit\n93: restrictions.  Therefore, we end up with a planning problem that contains\n94: lists of relations to be joined in any order, where any individual item\n95: might be a sub-list that has to be joined together before we can consider\n96: joining it to its siblings.  We process these sub-problems recursively,\n97: bottom up.  Note that the join list structure constrains the possible join\n98: orders, but it doesn't constrain the join implementation method at each\n99: join (nestloop, merge, hash), nor does it say which rel is considered outer\n100: or inner at each join.  We consider all these possibilities in building\n101: Paths. We generate a Path for each feasible join method, and select the\n102: cheapest Path.\n103: \n104: For each planning problem, therefore, we will have a list of relations\n105: that are either base rels or joinrels constructed per sub-join-lists.\n106: We can join these rels together in any order the planner sees fit.\n107: The standard (non-GEQO) planner does this as follows:\n108: \n109: Consider joining each RelOptInfo to each other RelOptInfo for which there\n110: is a usable joinclause, and generate a Path for each possible join method\n111: for each such pair.  (If we have a RelOptInfo with no join clauses, we have\n112: no choice but to generate a clauseless Cartesian-product join; so we\n113: consider joining that rel to each other available rel.  But in the presence\n114: of join clauses we will only consider joins that use available join\n115: clauses.  Note that join-order restrictions induced by outer joins and\n116: IN/EXISTS clauses are also checked, to ensure that we find a workable join\n117: order in cases where those restrictions force a clauseless join to be done.)\n118: \n119: If we only had two relations in the list, we are done: we just pick\n120: the cheapest path for the join RelOptInfo.  If we had more than two, we now\n121: need to consider ways of joining join RelOptInfos to each other to make\n122: join RelOptInfos that represent more than two list items.\n123: \n124: The join tree is constructed using a \"dynamic programming\" algorithm:\n125: in the first pass (already described) we consider ways to create join rels\n126: representing exactly two list items.  The second pass considers ways\n127: to make join rels that represent exactly three list items; the next pass,\n128: four items, etc.  The last pass considers how to make the final join\n129: relation that includes all list items --- obviously there can be only one\n130: join rel at this top level, whereas there can be more than one join rel\n131: at lower levels.  At each level we use joins that follow available join\n132: clauses, if possible, just as described for the first level.\n133: \n134: For example:\n135: \n136:     SELECT  *\n137:     FROM    tab1, tab2, tab3, tab4\n138:     WHERE   tab1.col = tab2.col AND\n139:         tab2.col = tab3.col AND\n140:         tab3.col = tab4.col\n141: \n142:     Tables 1, 2, 3, and 4 are joined as:\n143:     {1 2},{2 3},{3 4}\n144:     {1 2 3},{2 3 4}\n145:     {1 2 3 4}\n146: \n147:     SELECT  *\n148:     FROM    tab1, tab2, tab3, tab4\n149:     WHERE   tab1.col = tab2.col AND\n150:         tab1.col = tab3.col AND\n151:         tab1.col = tab4.col\n152: \n153:     Tables 1, 2, 3, and 4 are joined as:\n154:     {1 2},{1 3},{1 4}\n155:     {1 2 3},{1 3 4},{1 2 4}\n156:     {1 2 3 4}\n157: \n158: In each of these examples, other join-order possibilities will be excluded\n159: for lack of join clauses.  (In reality, use of EquivalenceClasses would\n160: allow us to deduce additional join clauses that allow more join orders,\n161: but here we ignore that to preserve the simplicity of the examples.)\n162: \n163: We consider left-handed plans (the outer rel of an upper join is a joinrel,\n164: but the inner is always a single list item); right-handed plans (outer rel\n165: is always a single item); and bushy plans (both inner and outer can be\n166: joins themselves).  For example, when building {1 2 3 4} we consider\n167: joining {1 2 3} to {4} (left-handed), {4} to {1 2 3} (right-handed), and\n168: {1 2} to {3 4} (bushy), among other choices.  Although the jointree\n169: scanning code produces these potential join combinations one at a time,\n170: all the ways to produce the same set of joined base rels will share the\n171: same RelOptInfo, so the paths produced from different join combinations\n172: that produce equivalent joinrels will compete in add_path().\n173: \n174: The dynamic-programming approach has an important property that's not\n175: immediately obvious: we will finish constructing all paths for a given\n176: relation before we construct any paths for relations containing that rel.\n177: This means that we can reliably identify the \"cheapest path\" for each rel\n178: before higher-level relations need to know that.  Also, we can safely\n179: discard a path when we find that another path for the same rel is better,\n180: without worrying that maybe there is already a reference to that path in\n181: some higher-level join path.  Without this, memory management for paths\n182: would be much more complicated.\n183: \n184: Once we have built the final join rel, we use either the cheapest path\n185: for it or the cheapest path with the desired ordering (if that's cheaper\n186: than applying a sort to the cheapest other path).\n187: \n188: If the query contains one-sided outer joins (LEFT or RIGHT joins), or\n189: IN or EXISTS WHERE clauses that were converted to semijoins or antijoins,\n190: then some of the possible join orders may be illegal.  These are excluded\n191: by having join_is_legal consult a side list of such \"special\" joins to see\n192: whether a proposed join is illegal.  (The same consultation allows it to\n193: see which join style should be applied for a valid join, ie, JOIN_INNER,\n194: JOIN_LEFT, etc.)\n195: \n196: \n197: Valid OUTER JOIN Optimizations\n198: ------------------------------\n199: \n200: The planner's treatment of outer join reordering is based on the following\n201: identities:\n202: \n203: 1.\t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) innerjoin C on (Pac)\n204: \t= (A innerjoin C on (Pac)) leftjoin B on (Pab)\n205: \n206: where Pac is a predicate referencing A and C, etc (in this case, clearly\n207: Pac cannot reference B, or the transformation is nonsensical).\n208: \n209: 2.\t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pac)\n210: \t= (A leftjoin C on (Pac)) leftjoin B on (Pab)\n211: \n212: 3.\t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pbc)\n213: \t= A leftjoin (B leftjoin C on (Pbc)) on (Pab)\n214: \n215: Identity 3 only holds if predicate Pbc must fail for all-null B rows\n216: (that is, Pbc is strict for at least one column of B).  If Pbc is not\n217: strict, the first form might produce some rows with nonnull C columns\n218: where the second form would make those entries null.\n219: \n220: RIGHT JOIN is equivalent to LEFT JOIN after switching the two input\n221: tables, so the same identities work for right joins.\n222: \n223: An example of a case that does *not* work is moving an innerjoin into or\n224: out of the nullable side of an outer join:\n225: \n226: \tA leftjoin (B join C on (Pbc)) on (Pab)\n227: \t!= (A leftjoin B on (Pab)) join C on (Pbc)\n228: \n229: SEMI joins work a little bit differently.  A semijoin can be reassociated\n230: into or out of the lefthand side of another semijoin, left join, or\n231: antijoin, but not into or out of the righthand side.  Likewise, an inner\n232: join, left join, or antijoin can be reassociated into or out of the\n233: lefthand side of a semijoin, but not into or out of the righthand side.\n234: \n235: ANTI joins work approximately like LEFT joins, except that identity 3\n236: fails if the join to C is an antijoin (even if Pbc is strict, and in\n237: both the cases where the other join is a leftjoin and where it is an\n238: antijoin).  So we can't reorder antijoins into or out of the RHS of a\n239: leftjoin or antijoin, even if the relevant clause is strict.\n240: \n241: The current code does not attempt to re-order FULL JOINs at all.\n242: FULL JOIN ordering is enforced by not collapsing FULL JOIN nodes when\n243: translating the jointree to \"joinlist\" representation.  Other types of\n244: JOIN nodes are normally collapsed so that they participate fully in the\n245: join order search.  To avoid generating illegal join orders, the planner\n246: creates a SpecialJoinInfo node for each non-inner join, and join_is_legal\n247: checks this list to decide if a proposed join is legal.\n248: \n249: What we store in SpecialJoinInfo nodes are the minimum sets of Relids\n250: required on each side of the join to form the outer join.  Note that\n251: these are minimums; there's no explicit maximum, since joining other\n252: rels to the OJ's syntactic rels may be legal.  Per identities 1 and 2,\n253: non-FULL joins can be freely associated into the lefthand side of an\n254: OJ, but in some cases they can't be associated into the righthand side.\n255: So the restriction enforced by join_is_legal is that a proposed join\n256: can't join a rel within or partly within an RHS boundary to one outside\n257: the boundary, unless the proposed join is a LEFT join that can associate\n258: into the SpecialJoinInfo's RHS using identity 3.\n259: \n260: The use of minimum Relid sets has some pitfalls; consider a query like\n261: \tA leftjoin (B leftjoin (C innerjoin D) on (Pbcd)) on Pa\n262: where Pa doesn't mention B/C/D at all.  In this case a naive computation\n263: would give the upper leftjoin's min LHS as {A} and min RHS as {C,D} (since\n264: we know that the innerjoin can't associate out of the leftjoin's RHS, and\n265: enforce that by including its relids in the leftjoin's min RHS).  And the\n266: lower leftjoin has min LHS of {B} and min RHS of {C,D}.  Given such\n267: information, join_is_legal would think it's okay to associate the upper\n268: join into the lower join's RHS, transforming the query to\n269: \tB leftjoin (A leftjoin (C innerjoin D) on Pa) on (Pbcd)\n270: which yields totally wrong answers.  We prevent that by forcing the min RHS\n271: for the upper join to include B.  This is perhaps overly restrictive, but\n272: such cases don't arise often so it's not clear that it's worth developing a\n273: more complicated system.\n274: \n275: \n276: Pulling Up Subqueries\n277: ---------------------\n278: \n279: As we described above, a subquery appearing in the range table is planned\n280: independently and treated as a \"black box\" during planning of the outer\n281: query.  This is necessary when the subquery uses features such as\n282: aggregates, GROUP, or DISTINCT.  But if the subquery is just a simple\n283: scan or join, treating the subquery as a black box may produce a poor plan\n284: compared to considering it as part of the entire plan search space.\n285: Therefore, at the start of the planning process the planner looks for\n286: simple subqueries and pulls them up into the main query's jointree.\n287: \n288: Pulling up a subquery may result in FROM-list joins appearing below the top\n289: of the join tree.  Each FROM-list is planned using the dynamic-programming\n290: search method described above.\n291: \n292: If pulling up a subquery produces a FROM-list as a direct child of another\n293: FROM-list, then we can merge the two FROM-lists together.  Once that's\n294: done, the subquery is an absolutely integral part of the outer query and\n295: will not constrain the join tree search space at all.  However, that could\n296: result in unpleasant growth of planning time, since the dynamic-programming\n297: search has runtime exponential in the number of FROM-items considered.\n298: Therefore, we don't merge FROM-lists if the result would have too many\n299: FROM-items in one list.\n300: \n301: \n302: Vars and PlaceHolderVars\n303: ------------------------\n304: \n305: A Var node is simply the parse-tree representation of a table column\n306: reference.  However, in the presence of outer joins, that concept is\n307: more subtle than it might seem.  We need to distinguish the values of\n308: a Var \"above\" and \"below\" any outer join that could force the Var to\n309: null.  As an example, consider\n310: \n311: \tSELECT * FROM t1 LEFT JOIN t2 ON (t1.x = t2.y) WHERE foo(t2.z)\n312: \n313: (Assume foo() is not strict, so that we can't reduce the left join to\n314: a plain join.)  A naive implementation might try to push the foo(t2.z)\n315: call down to the scan of t2, but that is not correct because\n316: (a) what foo() should actually see for a null-extended join row is NULL,\n317: and (b) if foo() returns false, we should suppress the t1 row from the\n318: join altogether, not emit it with a null-extended t2 row.  On the other\n319: hand, it *would* be correct (and desirable) to push that call down to\n320: the scan level if the query were\n321: \n322: \tSELECT * FROM t1 LEFT JOIN t2 ON (t1.x = t2.y AND foo(t2.z))\n323: \n324: This motivates considering \"t2.z\" within the left join's ON clause\n325: to be a different value from \"t2.z\" outside the JOIN clause.  The\n326: former can be identified with t2.z as seen at the relation scan level,\n327: but the latter can't.\n328: \n329: Another example occurs in connection with EquivalenceClasses (discussed\n330: below).  Given\n331: \n332: \tSELECT * FROM t1 LEFT JOIN t2 ON (t1.x = t2.y) WHERE t1.x = 42\n333: \n334: we would like to use the EquivalenceClass mechanisms to derive \"t2.y = 42\"\n335: to use as a restriction clause for the scan of t2.  (That works, because t2\n336: rows having y different from 42 cannot affect the query result.)  However,\n337: it'd be wrong to conclude that t2.y will be equal to t1.x in every joined\n338: row.  Part of the solution to this problem is to deem that \"t2.y\" in the\n339: ON clause refers to the relation-scan-level value of t2.y, but not to the\n340: value that y will have in joined rows, where it might be NULL rather than\n341: equal to t1.x.\n342: \n343: Therefore, Var nodes are decorated with \"varnullingrels\", which are sets\n344: of the rangetable indexes of outer joins that potentially null the Var\n345: at the point where it appears in the query.  (Using a set, not an ordered\n346: list, is fine since it doesn't matter which join forced the value to null;\n347: and that avoids having to change the representation when we consider\n348: different outer-join orders.)  In the examples above, all occurrences of\n349: t1.x would have empty varnullingrels, since the left join doesn't null t1.\n350: The t2 references within the JOIN ON clauses would also have empty\n351: varnullingrels.  But outside the JOIN clauses, any Vars referencing t2\n352: would have varnullingrels containing the index of the JOIN's rangetable\n353: entry (RTE), so that they'd be understood as potentially different from\n354: the t2 values seen at scan level.  Labeling t2.z in the WHERE clause with\n355: the JOIN's RT index lets us recognize that that occurrence of foo(t2.z)\n356: cannot be pushed down to the t2 scan level: we cannot evaluate that value\n357: at the scan level, but only after the join has been done.\n358: \n359: For LEFT and RIGHT outer joins, only Vars coming from the nullable side\n360: of the join are marked with that join's RT index.  For FULL joins, Vars\n361: from both inputs are marked.  (Such marking doesn't let us tell which\n362: side of the full join a Var came from; but that information can be found\n363: elsewhere at need.)\n364: \n365: Notionally, a Var having nonempty varnullingrels can be thought of as\n366: \tCASE WHEN any-of-these-outer-joins-produced-a-null-extended-row\n367: \t  THEN NULL\n368: \t  ELSE the-scan-level-value-of-the-column\n369: \t  END\n370: It's only notional, because no such calculation is ever done explicitly.\n371: In a finished plan, Vars occurring in scan-level plan nodes represent\n372: the actual table column values, but upper-level Vars are always\n373: references to outputs of lower-level plan nodes.  When a join node emits\n374: a null-extended row, it just returns nulls for the relevant output\n375: columns rather than copying up values from its input.  Because we don't\n376: ever have to do this calculation explicitly, it's not necessary to\n377: distinguish which side of an outer join got null-extended, which'd\n378: otherwise be essential information for FULL JOIN cases.\n379: \n380: Outer join identity 3 (discussed above) complicates this picture\n381: a bit.  In the form\n382: \tA leftjoin (B leftjoin C on (Pbc)) on (Pab)\n383: all of the Vars in clauses Pbc and Pab will have empty varnullingrels,\n384: but if we start with\n385: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pbc)\n386: then the parser will have marked Pbc's B Vars with the A/B join's\n387: RT index, making this form artificially different from the first.\n388: For discussion's sake, let's denote this marking with a star:\n389: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pb*c)\n390: To cope with this, once we have detected that commuting these joins\n391: is legal, we generate both the Pbc and Pb*c forms of that ON clause,\n392: by either removing or adding the first join's RT index in the B Vars\n393: that the parser created.  While generating paths for a plan step that\n394: joins B and C, we include as a relevant join qual only the form that\n395: is appropriate depending on whether A has already been joined to B.\n396: \n397: It's also worth noting that identity 3 makes \"the left join's RT index\"\n398: itself a bit of a fuzzy concept, since the syntactic scope of each join\n399: RTE will depend on which form was produced by the parser.  We resolve\n400: this by considering that a left join's identity is determined by its\n401: minimum set of right-hand-side input relations.  In both forms allowed\n402: by identity 3, we can identify the first join as having minimum RHS B\n403: and the second join as having minimum RHS C.\n404: \n405: Another thing to notice is that C Vars appearing outside the nested\n406: JOIN clauses will be marked as nulled by both left joins if the\n407: original parser input was in the first form of identity 3, but if the\n408: parser input was in the second form, such Vars will only be marked as\n409: nulled by the second join.  This is not really a semantic problem:\n410: such Vars will be marked the same way throughout the upper part of the\n411: query, so they will all look equal() which is correct; and they will not\n412: look equal() to any C Var appearing in the JOIN ON clause or below these\n413: joins.  However, when building Vars representing the outputs of join\n414: relations, we need to ensure that their varnullingrels are set to\n415: values consistent with the syntactic join order, so that they will\n416: appear equal() to pre-existing Vars in the upper part of the query.\n417: \n418: Outer joins also complicate handling of subquery pull-up.  Consider\n419: \n420: \tSELECT ..., ss.x FROM tab1\n421: \t  LEFT JOIN (SELECT *, 42 AS x FROM tab2) ss ON ...\n422: \n423: We want to be able to pull up the subquery as discussed previously,\n424: but we can't just replace the \"ss.x\" Var in the top-level SELECT list\n425: with the constant 42.  That'd result in always emitting 42, rather\n426: than emitting NULL in null-extended join rows.\n427: \n428: To solve this, we introduce the concept of PlaceHolderVars.\n429: A PlaceHolderVar is somewhat like a Var, in that its value originates\n430: at a relation scan level and can then be forced to null by higher-level\n431: outer joins; hence PlaceHolderVars carry a set of nulling rel IDs just\n432: like Vars.  Unlike a Var, whose original value comes from a table,\n433: a PlaceHolderVar's original value is defined by a query-determined\n434: expression (\"42\" in this example); so we represent the PlaceHolderVar\n435: as a node with that expression as child.  We insert a PlaceHolderVar\n436: whenever subquery pullup needs to replace a subquery-referencing Var\n437: that has nonempty varnullingrels with an expression that is not simply a\n438: Var.  (When the replacement expression is a pulled-up Var, we can just\n439: add the replaced Var's varnullingrels to its set.  Also, if the replaced\n440: Var has empty varnullingrels, we don't need a PlaceHolderVar: there is\n441: nothing that'd force the value to null, so the pulled-up expression is\n442: fine to use as-is.)  In a finished plan, a PlaceHolderVar becomes just\n443: the contained expression at whatever plan level it's supposed to be\n444: evaluated at, and then upper-level occurrences are replaced by Var\n445: references to that output column of the lower plan level.  That causes\n446: the value to go to null when appropriate at an outer join, in the same\n447: way as for normal Vars.  Thus, PlaceHolderVars are never seen outside\n448: the planner.\n449: \n450: PlaceHolderVars (PHVs) are more complicated than Vars in another way:\n451: their original value might need to be calculated at a join, not a\n452: base-level relation scan.  This can happen when a pulled-up subquery\n453: contains a join.  Because of this, a PHV can create a join order\n454: constraint that wouldn't otherwise exist, to ensure that it can\n455: be calculated before it is used.  A PHV's expression can also contain\n456: LATERAL references, adding complications that are discussed below.\n457: \n458: \n459: Relation Identification and Qual Clause Placement\n460: -------------------------------------------------\n461: \n462: A qual clause obtained from WHERE or JOIN/ON can be enforced at the lowest\n463: scan or join level that includes all relations used in the clause.  For\n464: this purpose we consider that outer joins listed in varnullingrels or\n465: phnullingrels are used in the clause, since we can't compute the qual's\n466: result correctly until we know whether such Vars have gone to null.\n467: \n468: The one exception to this general rule is that a non-degenerate outer\n469: JOIN/ON qual (one that references the non-nullable side of the join)\n470: cannot be enforced below that join, even if it doesn't reference the\n471: nullable side.  Pushing it down into the non-nullable side would result\n472: in rows disappearing from the join's result, rather than appearing as\n473: null-extended rows.  To handle that, when we identify such a qual we\n474: artificially add the join's minimum input relid set to the set of\n475: relations it is considered to use, forcing it to be evaluated exactly at\n476: that join level.  The same happens for outer-join quals that mention no\n477: relations at all.\n478: \n479: When attaching a qual clause to a join plan node that is performing an\n480: outer join, the qual clause is considered a \"join clause\" (that is, it is\n481: applied before the join performs null-extension) if it does not reference\n482: that outer join in any varnullingrels or phnullingrels set, or a \"filter\n483: clause\" (applied after null-extension) if it does reference that outer\n484: join.  A qual clause that originally appeared in that outer join's JOIN/ON\n485: will fall into the first category, since the parser would not have marked\n486: any of its Vars as referencing the outer join.  A qual clause that\n487: originally came from some upper ON clause or WHERE clause will be seen as\n488: referencing the outer join if it references any of the nullable side's\n489: Vars, since those Vars will be so marked by the parser.  But, if such a\n490: qual does not reference any nullable-side Vars, it's okay to push it down\n491: into the non-nullable side, so it won't get attached to the join node in\n492: the first place.\n493: \n494: These things lead us to identify join relations within the planner\n495: by the sets of base relation RT indexes plus outer join RT indexes\n496: that they include.  In that way, the sets of relations used by qual\n497: clauses can be directly compared to join relations' relid sets to\n498: see where to place the clauses.  These identifying sets are unique\n499: because, for any given collection of base relations, there is only\n500: one valid set of outer joins to have performed along the way to\n501: joining that set of base relations (although the order of applying\n502: them could vary, as discussed above).\n503: \n504: SEMI joins do not have RT indexes, because they are artifacts made by\n505: the planner rather than the parser.  (We could create rangetable\n506: entries for them, but there seems no need at present.)  This does not\n507: cause a problem for qual placement, because the nullable side of a\n508: semijoin is not referenceable from above the join, so there is never a\n509: need to cite it in varnullingrels or phnullingrels.  It does not cause a\n510: problem for join relation identification either, since whether a semijoin\n511: has been completed is again implicit in the set of base relations\n512: included in the join.\n513: \n514: As usual, outer join identity 3 complicates matters.  If we start with\n515: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pbc)\n516: then the parser will have marked any C Vars appearing above these joins\n517: with the RT index of the B/C join.  If we now transform to\n518: \tA leftjoin (B leftjoin C on (Pbc)) on (Pab)\n519: then it would appear that a clause using only such Vars could be pushed\n520: down and applied as a filter clause (not a join clause) at the lower\n521: B/C join.  But *this might not give the right answer* since the clause\n522: might see a non-null value for the C Var that will be replaced by null\n523: once the A/B join is performed.  We handle this by saying that the\n524: pushed-down join hasn't completely performed the work of the B/C join\n525: and hence is not entitled to include that outer join relid in its\n526: relid set.  When we form the A/B join, both outer joins' relids will\n527: be added to its relid set, and then the upper clause will be applied\n528: at the correct join level.  (Note there is no problem when identity 3\n529: is applied in the other direction: if we started with the second form\n530: then upper C Vars are marked with both outer join relids, so they\n531: cannot drop below whichever join is applied second.)  Similarly,\n532: Vars representing the output of a pushed-down join do not acquire\n533: nullingrel bits for that join until after the upper join is performed.\n534: \n535: There is one additional complication for qual clause placement, which\n536: occurs when we have made multiple versions of an outer-join clause as\n537: described previously (that is, we have both \"Pbc\" and \"Pb*c\" forms of\n538: the same clause seen in outer join identity 3).  When forming an outer\n539: join we only want to apply one of the redundant versions of the clause.\n540: If we are forming the B/C join without having yet computed the A/B\n541: join, it's easy to reject the \"Pb*c\" form since its required relid\n542: set includes the A/B join relid which is not in the input.  However,\n543: if we form B/C after A/B, then both forms of the clause are applicable\n544: so far as that test can tell.  We have to look more closely to notice\n545: that the \"Pbc\" clause form refers to relation B which is no longer\n546: directly accessible.  While such a check could be performed using the\n547: per-relation RelOptInfo.nulling_relids data, it would be annoyingly\n548: expensive to do over and over as we consider different join paths.\n549: To make this simple and reliable, we compute an \"incompatible_relids\"\n550: set for each variant version (clone) of a redundant clause.  A clone\n551: clause should not be applied if any of the outer-join relids listed in\n552: incompatible_relids has already been computed below the current join.\n553: \n554: \n555: Optimizer Functions\n556: -------------------\n557: \n558: The primary entry point is planner().\n559: \n560: planner()\n561: set up for recursive handling of subqueries\n562: -subquery_planner()\n563:  pull up sublinks and subqueries from rangetable, if possible\n564:  canonicalize qual\n565:      Attempt to simplify WHERE clause to the most useful form; this includes\n566:      flattening nested AND/ORs and detecting clauses that are duplicated in\n567:      different branches of an OR.\n568:  simplify constant expressions\n569:  process sublinks\n570:  convert Vars of outer query levels into Params\n571: --grouping_planner()\n572:   preprocess target list for non-SELECT queries\n573:   handle UNION/INTERSECT/EXCEPT, GROUP BY, HAVING, aggregates,\n574: \tORDER BY, DISTINCT, LIMIT\n575: ---query_planner()\n576:    make list of base relations used in query\n577:    split up the qual into restrictions (a=1) and joins (b=c)\n578:    find qual clauses that enable merge and hash joins\n579: ----make_one_rel()\n580:      set_base_rel_pathlists()\n581:       find seqscan and all index paths for each base relation\n582:       find selectivity of columns used in joins\n583:      make_rel_from_joinlist()\n584:       hand off join subproblems to a plugin, GEQO, or standard_join_search()\n585: ------standard_join_search()\n586:       call join_search_one_level() for each level of join tree needed\n587:       join_search_one_level():\n588:         For each joinrel of the prior level, do make_rels_by_clause_joins()\n589:         if it has join clauses, or make_rels_by_clauseless_joins() if not.\n590:         Also generate \"bushy plan\" joins between joinrels of lower levels.\n591:       Back at standard_join_search(), generate gather paths if needed for\n592:       each newly constructed joinrel, then apply set_cheapest() to extract\n593:       the cheapest path for it.\n594:       Loop back if this wasn't the top join level.\n595:   Back at grouping_planner:\n596:   do grouping (GROUP BY) and aggregation\n597:   do window functions\n598:   make unique (DISTINCT)\n599:   do sorting (ORDER BY)\n600:   do limit (LIMIT/OFFSET)\n601: Back at planner():\n602: convert finished Path tree into a Plan tree\n603: do final cleanup after planning\n604: \n605: \n606: Optimizer Data Structures\n607: -------------------------\n608: \n609: PlannerGlobal   - global information for a single planner invocation\n610: \n611: PlannerInfo     - information for planning a particular Query (we make\n612:                   a separate PlannerInfo node for each sub-Query)\n613: \n614: RelOptInfo      - a relation or joined relations\n615: \n616:  RestrictInfo   - WHERE clauses, like \"x = 3\" or \"y = z\"\n617:                   (note the same structure is used for restriction and\n618:                    join clauses)\n619: \n620:  Path           - every way to generate a RelOptInfo(sequential,index,joins)\n621:   A plain Path node can represent several simple plans, per its pathtype:\n622:     T_SeqScan   - sequential scan\n623:     T_SampleScan - tablesample scan\n624:     T_FunctionScan - function-in-FROM scan\n625:     T_TableFuncScan - table function scan\n626:     T_ValuesScan - VALUES scan\n627:     T_CteScan   - CTE (WITH) scan\n628:     T_NamedTuplestoreScan - ENR scan\n629:     T_WorkTableScan - scan worktable of a recursive CTE\n630:     T_Result    - childless Result plan node (used for FROM-less SELECT)\n631:   IndexPath     - index scan\n632:   BitmapHeapPath - top of a bitmapped index scan\n633:   TidPath       - scan by CTID\n634:   TidRangePath  - scan a contiguous range of CTIDs\n635:   SubqueryScanPath - scan a subquery-in-FROM\n636:   ForeignPath   - scan a foreign table, foreign join or foreign upper-relation\n637:   CustomPath    - for custom scan providers\n638:   AppendPath    - append multiple subpaths together\n639:   MergeAppendPath - merge multiple subpaths, preserving their common sort order\n640:   GroupResultPath - childless Result plan node (used for degenerate grouping)\n641:   MaterialPath  - a Material plan node\n642:   MemoizePath   - a Memoize plan node for caching tuples from sub-paths\n643:   UniquePath    - remove duplicate rows (either by hashing or sorting)\n644:   GatherPath    - collect the results of parallel workers\n645:   GatherMergePath - collect parallel results, preserving their common sort order\n646:   ProjectionPath - a Result plan node with child (used for projection)\n647:   ProjectSetPath - a ProjectSet plan node applied to some sub-path\n648:   SortPath      - a Sort plan node applied to some sub-path\n649:   IncrementalSortPath - an IncrementalSort plan node applied to some sub-path\n650:   GroupPath     - a Group plan node applied to some sub-path\n651:   UpperUniquePath - a Unique plan node applied to some sub-path\n652:   AggPath       - an Agg plan node applied to some sub-path\n653:   GroupingSetsPath - an Agg plan node used to implement GROUPING SETS\n654:   MinMaxAggPath - a Result plan node with subplans performing MIN/MAX\n655:   WindowAggPath - a WindowAgg plan node applied to some sub-path\n656:   SetOpPath     - a SetOp plan node applied to two sub-paths\n657:   RecursiveUnionPath - a RecursiveUnion plan node applied to two sub-paths\n658:   LockRowsPath  - a LockRows plan node applied to some sub-path\n659:   ModifyTablePath - a ModifyTable plan node applied to some sub-path(s)\n660:   LimitPath     - a Limit plan node applied to some sub-path\n661:   NestPath      - nested-loop joins\n662:   MergePath     - merge joins\n663:   HashPath      - hash joins\n664: \n665:  EquivalenceClass - a data structure representing a set of values known equal\n666: \n667:  PathKey        - a data structure representing the sort ordering of a path\n668: \n669: The optimizer spends a good deal of its time worrying about the ordering\n670: of the tuples returned by a path.  The reason this is useful is that by\n671: knowing the sort ordering of a path, we may be able to use that path as\n672: the left or right input of a mergejoin and avoid an explicit sort step.\n673: Nestloops and hash joins don't really care what the order of their inputs\n674: is, but mergejoin needs suitably ordered inputs.  Therefore, all paths\n675: generated during the optimization process are marked with their sort order\n676: (to the extent that it is known) for possible use by a higher-level merge.\n677: \n678: It is also possible to avoid an explicit sort step to implement a user's\n679: ORDER BY clause if the final path has the right ordering already, so the\n680: sort ordering is of interest even at the top level.  grouping_planner() will\n681: look for the cheapest path with a sort order matching the desired order,\n682: then compare its cost to the cost of using the cheapest-overall path and\n683: doing an explicit sort on that.\n684: \n685: When we are generating paths for a particular RelOptInfo, we discard a path\n686: if it is more expensive than another known path that has the same or better\n687: sort order.  We will never discard a path that is the only known way to\n688: achieve a given sort order (without an explicit sort, that is).  In this\n689: way, the next level up will have the maximum freedom to build mergejoins\n690: without sorting, since it can pick from any of the paths retained for its\n691: inputs.\n692: \n693: \n694: EquivalenceClasses\n695: ------------------\n696: \n697: During the deconstruct_jointree() scan of the query's qual clauses, we\n698: look for mergejoinable equality clauses A = B.  When we find one, we\n699: create an EquivalenceClass containing the expressions A and B to record\n700: that they are equal.  If we later find another equivalence clause B = C,\n701: we add C to the existing EquivalenceClass for {A B}; this may require\n702: merging two existing EquivalenceClasses.  At the end of the scan, we have\n703: sets of values that are known all transitively equal to each other.  We can\n704: therefore use a comparison of any pair of the values as a restriction or\n705: join clause (when these values are available at the scan or join, of\n706: course); furthermore, we need test only one such comparison, not all of\n707: them.  Therefore, equivalence clauses are removed from the standard qual\n708: distribution process.  Instead, when preparing a restriction or join clause\n709: list, we examine each EquivalenceClass to see if it can contribute a\n710: clause, and if so we select an appropriate pair of values to compare.  For\n711: example, if we are trying to join A's relation to C's, we can generate the\n712: clause A = C, even though this appeared nowhere explicitly in the original\n713: query.  This may allow us to explore join paths that otherwise would have\n714: been rejected as requiring Cartesian-product joins.\n715: \n716: Sometimes an EquivalenceClass may contain a pseudo-constant expression\n717: (i.e., one not containing Vars or Aggs of the current query level, nor\n718: volatile functions).  In this case we do not follow the policy of\n719: dynamically generating join clauses: instead, we dynamically generate\n720: restriction clauses \"var = const\" wherever one of the variable members of\n721: the class can first be computed.  For example, if we have A = B and B = 42,\n722: we effectively generate the restriction clauses A = 42 and B = 42, and then\n723: we need not bother with explicitly testing the join clause A = B when the\n724: relations are joined.  In effect, all the class members can be tested at\n725: relation-scan level and there's never a need for join tests.\n726: \n727: The precise technical interpretation of an EquivalenceClass is that it\n728: asserts that at any plan node where more than one of its member values\n729: can be computed, output rows in which the values are not all equal may\n730: be discarded without affecting the query result.  (We require all levels\n731: of the plan to enforce EquivalenceClasses, hence a join need not recheck\n732: equality of values that were computable by one of its children.)\n733: \n734: Outer joins complicate this picture quite a bit, however.  While we could\n735: theoretically use mergejoinable equality clauses that appear in outer-join\n736: conditions as sources of EquivalenceClasses, there's a serious difficulty:\n737: the resulting deductions are not valid everywhere.  For example, given\n738: \n739: \tSELECT * FROM a LEFT JOIN b ON (a.x = b.y AND a.x = 42);\n740: \n741: we can safely derive b.y = 42 and use that in the scan of B, because B\n742: rows not having b.y = 42 will not contribute to the join result.  However,\n743: we cannot apply a.x = 42 at the scan of A, or we will remove rows that\n744: should appear in the join result.  We could apply a.x = 42 as an outer join\n745: condition (and then it would be unnecessary to also check a.x = b.y).\n746: This is not yet implemented, however.\n747: \n748: A related issue is that constants appearing below an outer join are\n749: less constant than they appear.  Ordinarily, if we find \"A = 1\" and\n750: \"B = 1\", it's okay to put A and B into the same EquivalenceClass.\n751: But consider\n752: \n753: \tSELECT * FROM a\n754: \t  LEFT JOIN (SELECT * FROM b WHERE b.z = 1) b ON (a.x = b.y)\n755: \tWHERE a.x = 1;\n756: \n757: It would be a serious error to conclude that a.x = b.z, so we cannot\n758: form a single EquivalenceClass {a.x b.z 1}.\n759: \n760: This leads to considering EquivalenceClasses as applying within \"join\n761: domains\", which are sets of relations that are inner-joined to each other.\n762: (We can treat semijoins as if they were inner joins for this purpose.)\n763: There is a top-level join domain, and then each outer join in the query\n764: creates a new join domain comprising its nullable side.  Full joins create\n765: two join domains, one for each side.  EquivalenceClasses generated from\n766: WHERE are associated with the top-level join domain.  EquivalenceClasses\n767: generated from the ON clause of an outer join are associated with the\n768: domain created by that outer join.  EquivalenceClasses generated from the\n769: ON clause of an inner or semi join are associated with the syntactically\n770: most closely nested join domain.\n771: \n772: Having defined these domains, we can fix the not-so-constant-constants\n773: problem by considering that constants only match EquivalenceClass members\n774: when they come from clauses within the same join domain.  In the above\n775: example, this means we keep {a.x 1} and {b.z 1} as separate\n776: EquivalenceClasses and don't erroneously merge them.  We don't have to\n777: worry about this for Vars (or expressions containing Vars), because\n778: references to the \"same\" column from different join domains will have\n779: different varnullingrels and thus won't be equal() anyway.\n780: \n781: In the future, the join-domain concept may allow us to treat mergejoinable\n782: outer-join conditions as sources of EquivalenceClasses.  The idea would be\n783: that conditions derived from such classes could only be enforced at scans\n784: or joins that are within the appropriate join domain.  This is not\n785: implemented yet, however, as the details are trickier than they appear.\n786: \n787: Another instructive example is:\n788: \n789: \tSELECT *\n790: \t  FROM a LEFT JOIN\n791: \t       (SELECT * FROM b JOIN c ON b.y = c.z WHERE b.y = 10) ss\n792: \t       ON a.x = ss.y\n793: \t  ORDER BY ss.y;\n794: \n795: We can form the EquivalenceClass {b.y c.z 10} and thereby apply c.z = 10\n796: while scanning C, as well as b.y = 10 while scanning B, so that no clause\n797: needs to be checked at the inner join.  The left-join clause \"a.x = ss.y\"\n798: (really \"a.x = b.y\") is not considered an equivalence clause, so we do\n799: not insert a.x into that same EquivalenceClass; if we did, we'd falsely\n800: conclude a.x = 10.  In the future though we might be able to do that,\n801: if we can keep from applying a.x = 10 at the scan of A, which in principle\n802: we could do by noting that the EquivalenceClass only applies within the\n803: {B,C} join domain.\n804: \n805: Also notice that ss.y in the ORDER BY is really b.y* (that is, the\n806: possibly-nulled form of b.y), so we will not confuse it with the b.y member\n807: of the lower EquivalenceClass.  Thus, we won't mistakenly conclude that\n808: that ss.y is equal to a constant, which if true would lead us to think that\n809: sorting for the ORDER BY is unnecessary (see discussion of PathKeys below).\n810: Instead, there will be a separate EquivalenceClass containing only b.y*,\n811: which will form the basis for the PathKey describing the required sort\n812: order.\n813: \n814: Also consider this variant:\n815: \n816: \tSELECT *\n817: \t  FROM a LEFT JOIN\n818: \t       (SELECT * FROM b JOIN c ON b.y = c.z WHERE b.y = 10) ss\n819: \t       ON a.x = ss.y\n820: \t  WHERE a.x = 42;\n821: \n822: We still form the EquivalenceClass {b.y c.z 10}, and additionally\n823: we have an EquivalenceClass {a.x 42} belonging to a different join domain.\n824: We cannot use \"a.x = b.y\" to merge these classes.  However, we can compare\n825: that outer join clause to the existing EquivalenceClasses and form the\n826: derived clause \"b.y = 42\", which we can treat as a valid equivalence\n827: within the lower join domain (since no row of that domain not having\n828: b.y = 42 can contribute to the outer-join result).  That makes the lower\n829: EquivalenceClass {42 b.y c.z 10}, resulting in the contradiction 10 = 42,\n830: which lets the planner deduce that the B/C join need not be computed at\n831: all: the result of that whole join domain can be forced to empty.\n832: (This gets implemented as a gating Result filter, since more usually the\n833: potential contradiction involves Param values rather than just Consts, and\n834: thus it has to be checked at runtime.  We can use the join domain to\n835: determine the join level at which to place the gating condition.)\n836: \n837: There is an additional complication when re-ordering outer joins according\n838: to identity 3.  Recall that the two choices we consider for such joins are\n839: \n840: \tA leftjoin (B leftjoin C on (Pbc)) on (Pab)\n841: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (Pb*c)\n842: \n843: where the star denotes varnullingrels markers on B's Vars.  When Pbc\n844: is (or includes) a mergejoinable clause, we have something like\n845: \n846: \tA leftjoin (B leftjoin C on (b.b = c.c)) on (Pab)\n847: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) leftjoin C on (b.b* = c.c)\n848: \n849: We could generate an EquivalenceClause linking b.b and c.c, but if we\n850: then also try to link b.b* and c.c, we end with a nonsensical conclusion\n851: that b.b and b.b* are equal (at least in some parts of the plan tree).\n852: In any case, the conclusions we could derive from such a thing would be\n853: largely duplicative.  Conditions involving b.b* can't be computed below\n854: this join nest, while any conditions that can be computed would be\n855: duplicative of what we'd get from the b.b/c.c combination.  Therefore,\n856: we choose to generate an EquivalenceClause linking b.b and c.c, but\n857: \"b.b* = c.c\" is handled as just an ordinary clause.\n858: \n859: To aid in determining the sort ordering(s) that can work with a mergejoin,\n860: we mark each mergejoinable clause with the EquivalenceClasses of its left\n861: and right inputs.  For an equivalence clause, these are of course the same\n862: EquivalenceClass.  For a non-equivalence mergejoinable clause (such as an\n863: outer-join qualification), we generate two separate EquivalenceClasses for\n864: the left and right inputs.  This may result in creating single-item\n865: equivalence \"classes\", though of course these are still subject to merging\n866: if other equivalence clauses are later found to bear on the same\n867: expressions.\n868: \n869: Another way that we may form a single-item EquivalenceClass is in creation\n870: of a PathKey to represent a desired sort order (see below).  This happens\n871: if an ORDER BY or GROUP BY key is not mentioned in any equivalence\n872: clause.  We need to reason about sort orders in such queries, and our\n873: representation of sort ordering is a PathKey which depends on an\n874: EquivalenceClass, so we have to make an EquivalenceClass.  This is a bit\n875: different from the above cases because such an EquivalenceClass might\n876: contain an aggregate function or volatile expression.  (A clause containing\n877: a volatile function will never be considered mergejoinable, even if its top\n878: operator is mergejoinable, so there is no way for a volatile expression to\n879: get into EquivalenceClasses otherwise.  Aggregates are disallowed in WHERE\n880: altogether, so will never be found in a mergejoinable clause.)  This is just\n881: a convenience to maintain a uniform PathKey representation: such an\n882: EquivalenceClass will never be merged with any other.  Note in particular\n883: that a single-item EquivalenceClass {a.x} is *not* meant to imply an\n884: assertion that a.x = a.x; the practical effect of this is that a.x could\n885: be NULL.\n886: \n887: An EquivalenceClass also contains a list of btree opfamily OIDs, which\n888: determines what the equalities it represents actually \"mean\".  All the\n889: equivalence clauses that contribute to an EquivalenceClass must have\n890: equality operators that belong to the same set of opfamilies.  (Note: most\n891: of the time, a particular equality operator belongs to only one family, but\n892: it's possible that it belongs to more than one.  We keep track of all the\n893: families to ensure that we can make use of an index belonging to any one of\n894: the families for mergejoin purposes.)\n895: \n896: For the same sort of reason, an EquivalenceClass is also associated\n897: with a particular collation, if its datatype(s) care about collation.\n898: \n899: An EquivalenceClass can contain \"em_is_child\" members, which are copies\n900: of members that contain appendrel parent relation Vars, transposed to\n901: contain the equivalent child-relation variables or expressions.  These\n902: members are *not* full-fledged members of the EquivalenceClass and do not\n903: affect the class's overall properties at all.  They are kept only to\n904: simplify matching of child-relation expressions to EquivalenceClasses.\n905: Most operations on EquivalenceClasses needn't look at child members.\n906: \n907: \n908: PathKeys\n909: --------\n910: \n911: The PathKeys data structure represents what is known about the sort order\n912: of the tuples generated by a particular Path.  A path's pathkeys field is a\n913: list of PathKey nodes, where the n'th item represents the n'th sort key of\n914: the result.  Each PathKey contains these fields:\n915: \n916: \t* a reference to an EquivalenceClass\n917: \t* a btree opfamily OID (must match one of those in the EC)\n918: \t* a sort direction (ascending or descending)\n919: \t* a nulls-first-or-last flag\n920: \n921: The EquivalenceClass represents the value being sorted on.  Since the\n922: various members of an EquivalenceClass are known equal according to the\n923: opfamily, we can consider a path sorted by any one of them to be sorted by\n924: any other too; this is what justifies referencing the whole\n925: EquivalenceClass rather than just one member of it.\n926: \n927: In single/base relation RelOptInfo's, the Paths represent various ways\n928: of scanning the relation and the resulting ordering of the tuples.\n929: Sequential scan Paths have NIL pathkeys, indicating no known ordering.\n930: Index scans have Path.pathkeys that represent the chosen index's ordering,\n931: if any.  A single-key index would create a single-PathKey list, while a\n932: multi-column index generates a list with one element per key index column.\n933: Non-key columns specified in the INCLUDE clause of covering indexes don't\n934: have corresponding PathKeys in the list, because they have no influence on\n935: index ordering.  (Actually, since an index can be scanned either forward or\n936: backward, there are two possible sort orders and two possible PathKey lists\n937: it can generate.)\n938: \n939: Note that a bitmap scan has NIL pathkeys since we can say nothing about\n940: the overall order of its result.  Also, an indexscan on an unordered type\n941: of index generates NIL pathkeys.  However, we can always create a pathkey\n942: by doing an explicit sort.  The pathkeys for a Sort plan's output just\n943: represent the sort key fields and the ordering operators used.\n944: \n945: Things get more interesting when we consider joins.  Suppose we do a\n946: mergejoin between A and B using the mergeclause A.X = B.Y.  The output\n947: of the mergejoin is sorted by X --- but it is also sorted by Y.  Again,\n948: this can be represented by a PathKey referencing an EquivalenceClass\n949: containing both X and Y.\n950: \n951: With a little further thought, it becomes apparent that nestloop joins\n952: can also produce sorted output.  For example, if we do a nestloop join\n953: between outer relation A and inner relation B, then any pathkeys relevant\n954: to A are still valid for the join result: we have not altered the order of\n955: the tuples from A.  Even more interesting, if there was an equivalence clause\n956: A.X=B.Y, and A.X was a pathkey for the outer relation A, then we can assert\n957: that B.Y is a pathkey for the join result; X was ordered before and still\n958: is, and the joined values of Y are equal to the joined values of X, so Y\n959: must now be ordered too.  This is true even though we used neither an\n960: explicit sort nor a mergejoin on Y.  (Note: hash joins cannot be counted\n961: on to preserve the order of their outer relation, because the executor\n962: might decide to \"batch\" the join, so we always set pathkeys to NIL for\n963: a hashjoin path.)\n964: \n965: An outer join doesn't preserve the ordering of its nullable input\n966: relation(s), because it might insert nulls at random points in the\n967: ordering.  We don't need to think about this explicitly in the PathKey\n968: representation, because a PathKey representing a post-join variable\n969: will contain varnullingrel bits, making it not equal to a PathKey\n970: representing the pre-join value.\n971: \n972: In general, we can justify using EquivalenceClasses as the basis for\n973: pathkeys because, whenever we scan a relation containing multiple\n974: EquivalenceClass members or join two relations each containing\n975: EquivalenceClass members, we apply restriction or join clauses derived from\n976: the EquivalenceClass.  This guarantees that any two values listed in the\n977: EquivalenceClass are in fact equal in all tuples emitted by the scan or\n978: join, and therefore that if the tuples are sorted by one of the values,\n979: they can be considered sorted by any other as well.  It does not matter\n980: whether the test clause is used as a mergeclause, or merely enforced\n981: after-the-fact as a qpqual filter.\n982: \n983: Note that there is no particular difficulty in labeling a path's sort\n984: order with a PathKey referencing an EquivalenceClass that contains\n985: variables not yet joined into the path's output.  We can simply ignore\n986: such entries as not being relevant (yet).  This makes it possible to\n987: use the same EquivalenceClasses throughout the join planning process.\n988: In fact, by being careful not to generate multiple identical PathKey\n989: objects, we can reduce comparison of EquivalenceClasses and PathKeys\n990: to simple pointer comparison, which is a huge savings because add_path\n991: has to make a large number of PathKey comparisons in deciding whether\n992: competing Paths are equivalently sorted.\n993: \n994: Pathkeys are also useful to represent an ordering that we wish to achieve,\n995: since they are easily compared to the pathkeys of a potential candidate\n996: path.  So, SortGroupClause lists are turned into pathkeys lists for use\n997: inside the optimizer.\n998: \n999: An additional refinement we can make is to insist that canonical pathkey\n1000: lists (sort orderings) do not mention the same EquivalenceClass more than\n1001: once.  For example, in all these cases the second sort column is redundant,\n1002: because it cannot distinguish values that are the same according to the\n1003: first sort column:\n1004: \tSELECT ... ORDER BY x, x\n1005: \tSELECT ... ORDER BY x, x DESC\n1006: \tSELECT ... WHERE x = y ORDER BY x, y\n1007: Although a user probably wouldn't write \"ORDER BY x,x\" directly, such\n1008: redundancies are more probable once equivalence classes have been\n1009: considered.  Also, the system may generate redundant pathkey lists when\n1010: computing the sort ordering needed for a mergejoin.  By eliminating the\n1011: redundancy, we save time and improve planning, since the planner will more\n1012: easily recognize equivalent orderings as being equivalent.\n1013: \n1014: Another interesting property is that if the underlying EquivalenceClass\n1015: contains a constant, then the pathkey is completely redundant and need not\n1016: be sorted by at all!  Every interesting row must contain the same value,\n1017: so there's no need to sort.  This might seem pointless because users\n1018: are unlikely to write \"... WHERE x = 42 ORDER BY x\", but it allows us to\n1019: recognize when particular index columns are irrelevant to the sort order:\n1020: if we have \"... WHERE x = 42 ORDER BY y\", scanning an index on (x,y)\n1021: produces correctly ordered data without a sort step.  We used to have very\n1022: ugly ad-hoc code to recognize that in limited contexts, but discarding\n1023: constant ECs from pathkeys makes it happen cleanly and automatically.\n1024: \n1025: \n1026: Order of processing for EquivalenceClasses and PathKeys\n1027: -------------------------------------------------------\n1028: \n1029: As alluded to above, there is a specific sequence of phases in the\n1030: processing of EquivalenceClasses and PathKeys during planning.  During the\n1031: initial scanning of the query's quals (deconstruct_jointree followed by\n1032: reconsider_outer_join_clauses), we construct EquivalenceClasses based on\n1033: mergejoinable clauses found in the quals.  At the end of this process,\n1034: we know all we can know about equivalence of different variables, so\n1035: subsequently there will be no further merging of EquivalenceClasses.\n1036: At that point it is possible to consider the EquivalenceClasses as\n1037: \"canonical\" and build canonical PathKeys that reference them.  At this\n1038: time we construct PathKeys for the query's ORDER BY and related clauses.\n1039: (Any ordering expressions that do not appear elsewhere will result in\n1040: the creation of new EquivalenceClasses, but this cannot result in merging\n1041: existing classes, so canonical-ness is not lost.)\n1042: \n1043: Because all the EquivalenceClasses are known before we begin path\n1044: generation, we can use them as a guide to which indexes are of interest:\n1045: if an index's column is not mentioned in any EquivalenceClass then that\n1046: index's sort order cannot possibly be helpful for the query.  This allows\n1047: short-circuiting of much of the processing of create_index_paths() for\n1048: irrelevant indexes.\n1049: \n1050: There are some cases where planner.c constructs additional\n1051: EquivalenceClasses and PathKeys after query_planner has completed.\n1052: In these cases, the extra ECs/PKs are needed to represent sort orders\n1053: that were not considered during query_planner.  Such situations should be\n1054: minimized since it is impossible for query_planner to return a plan\n1055: producing such a sort order, meaning an explicit sort will always be needed.\n1056: Currently this happens only for queries involving multiple window functions\n1057: with different orderings, for which extra sorts are needed anyway.\n1058: \n1059: \n1060: Parameterized Paths\n1061: -------------------\n1062: \n1063: The naive way to join two relations using a clause like WHERE A.X = B.Y\n1064: is to generate a nestloop plan like this:\n1065: \n1066: \tNestLoop\n1067: \t\tFilter: A.X = B.Y\n1068: \t\t-> Seq Scan on A\n1069: \t\t-> Seq Scan on B\n1070: \n1071: We can make this better by using a merge or hash join, but it still\n1072: requires scanning all of both input relations.  If A is very small and B is\n1073: very large, but there is an index on B.Y, it can be enormously better to do\n1074: something like this:\n1075: \n1076: \tNestLoop\n1077: \t\t-> Seq Scan on A\n1078: \t\t-> Index Scan using B_Y_IDX on B\n1079: \t\t\tIndex Condition: B.Y = A.X\n1080: \n1081: Here, we are expecting that for each row scanned from A, the nestloop\n1082: plan node will pass down the current value of A.X into the scan of B.\n1083: That allows the indexscan to treat A.X as a constant for any one\n1084: invocation, and thereby use it as an index key.  This is the only plan type\n1085: that can avoid fetching all of B, and for small numbers of rows coming from\n1086: A, that will dominate every other consideration.  (As A gets larger, this\n1087: gets less attractive, and eventually a merge or hash join will win instead.\n1088: So we have to cost out all the alternatives to decide what to do.)\n1089: \n1090: It can be useful for the parameter value to be passed down through\n1091: intermediate layers of joins, for example:\n1092: \n1093: \tNestLoop\n1094: \t\t-> Seq Scan on A\n1095: \t\tHash Join\n1096: \t\t\tJoin Condition: B.Y = C.W\n1097: \t\t\t-> Seq Scan on B\n1098: \t\t\t-> Index Scan using C_Z_IDX on C\n1099: \t\t\t\tIndex Condition: C.Z = A.X\n1100: \n1101: If all joins are plain inner joins then this is usually unnecessary,\n1102: because it's possible to reorder the joins so that a parameter is used\n1103: immediately below the nestloop node that provides it.  But in the\n1104: presence of outer joins, such join reordering may not be possible.\n1105: \n1106: Also, the bottom-level scan might require parameters from more than one\n1107: other relation.  In principle we could join the other relations first\n1108: so that all the parameters are supplied from a single nestloop level.\n1109: But if those other relations have no join clause in common (which is\n1110: common in star-schema queries for instance), the planner won't consider\n1111: joining them directly to each other.  In such a case we need to be able\n1112: to create a plan like\n1113: \n1114:     NestLoop\n1115:         -> Seq Scan on SmallTable1 A\n1116:         NestLoop\n1117:             -> Seq Scan on SmallTable2 B\n1118:             -> Index Scan using XYIndex on LargeTable C\n1119:                  Index Condition: C.X = A.AID and C.Y = B.BID\n1120: \n1121: so we should be willing to pass down A.AID through a join even though\n1122: there is no join order constraint forcing the plan to look like this.\n1123: \n1124: Before version 9.2, Postgres used ad-hoc methods for planning and\n1125: executing nestloop queries of this kind, and those methods could not\n1126: handle passing parameters down through multiple join levels.\n1127: \n1128: To plan such queries, we now use a notion of a \"parameterized path\",\n1129: which is a path that makes use of a join clause to a relation that's not\n1130: scanned by the path.  In the example two above, we would construct a\n1131: path representing the possibility of doing this:\n1132: \n1133: \t-> Index Scan using C_Z_IDX on C\n1134: \t\tIndex Condition: C.Z = A.X\n1135: \n1136: This path will be marked as being parameterized by relation A.  (Note that\n1137: this is only one of the possible access paths for C; we'd still have a\n1138: plain unparameterized seqscan, and perhaps other possibilities.)  The\n1139: parameterization marker does not prevent joining the path to B, so one of\n1140: the paths generated for the joinrel {B C} will represent\n1141: \n1142: \tHash Join\n1143: \t\tJoin Condition: B.Y = C.W\n1144: \t\t-> Seq Scan on B\n1145: \t\t-> Index Scan using C_Z_IDX on C\n1146: \t\t\tIndex Condition: C.Z = A.X\n1147: \n1148: This path is still marked as being parameterized by A.  When we attempt to\n1149: join {B C} to A to form the complete join tree, such a path can only be\n1150: used as the inner side of a nestloop join: it will be ignored for other\n1151: possible join types.  So we will form a join path representing the query\n1152: plan shown above, and it will compete in the usual way with paths built\n1153: from non-parameterized scans.\n1154: \n1155: While all ordinary paths for a particular relation generate the same set\n1156: of rows (since they must all apply the same set of restriction clauses),\n1157: parameterized paths typically generate fewer rows than less-parameterized\n1158: paths, since they have additional clauses to work with.  This means we\n1159: must consider the number of rows generated as an additional figure of\n1160: merit.  A path that costs more than another, but generates fewer rows,\n1161: must be kept since the smaller number of rows might save work at some\n1162: intermediate join level.  (It would not save anything if joined\n1163: immediately to the source of the parameters.)\n1164: \n1165: To keep cost estimation rules relatively simple, we make an implementation\n1166: restriction that all paths for a given relation of the same parameterization\n1167: (i.e., the same set of outer relations supplying parameters) must have the\n1168: same rowcount estimate.  This is justified by insisting that each such path\n1169: apply *all* join clauses that are available with the named outer relations.\n1170: Different paths might, for instance, choose different join clauses to use\n1171: as index clauses; but they must then apply any other join clauses available\n1172: from the same outer relations as filter conditions, so that the set of rows\n1173: returned is held constant.  This restriction doesn't degrade the quality of\n1174: the finished plan: it amounts to saying that we should always push down\n1175: movable join clauses to the lowest possible evaluation level, which is a\n1176: good thing anyway.  The restriction is useful in particular to support\n1177: pre-filtering of join paths in add_path_precheck.  Without this rule we\n1178: could never reject a parameterized path in advance of computing its rowcount\n1179: estimate, which would greatly reduce the value of the pre-filter mechanism.\n1180: \n1181: To limit planning time, we have to avoid generating an unreasonably large\n1182: number of parameterized paths.  We do this by only generating parameterized\n1183: relation scan paths for index scans, and then only for indexes for which\n1184: suitable join clauses are available.  There are also heuristics in join\n1185: planning that try to limit the number of parameterized paths considered.\n1186: \n1187: In particular, there's been a deliberate policy decision to favor hash\n1188: joins over merge joins for parameterized join steps (those occurring below\n1189: a nestloop that provides parameters to the lower join's inputs).  While we\n1190: do not ignore merge joins entirely, joinpath.c does not fully explore the\n1191: space of potential merge joins with parameterized inputs.  Also, add_path\n1192: treats parameterized paths as having no pathkeys, so that they compete\n1193: only on cost and rowcount; they don't get preference for producing a\n1194: special sort order.  This creates additional bias against merge joins,\n1195: since we might discard a path that could have been useful for performing\n1196: a merge without an explicit sort step.  Since a parameterized path must\n1197: ultimately be used on the inside of a nestloop, where its sort order is\n1198: uninteresting, these choices do not affect any requirement for the final\n1199: output order of a query --- they only make it harder to use a merge join\n1200: at a lower level.  The savings in planning work justifies that.\n1201: \n1202: Similarly, parameterized paths do not normally get preference in add_path\n1203: for having cheap startup cost; that's seldom of much value when on the\n1204: inside of a nestloop, so it seems not worth keeping extra paths solely for\n1205: that.  An exception occurs for parameterized paths for the RHS relation of\n1206: a SEMI or ANTI join: in those cases, we can stop the inner scan after the\n1207: first match, so it's primarily startup not total cost that we care about.\n1208: \n1209: \n1210: LATERAL subqueries\n1211: ------------------\n1212: \n1213: As of 9.3 we support SQL-standard LATERAL references from subqueries in\n1214: FROM (and also functions in FROM).  The planner implements these by\n1215: generating parameterized paths for any RTE that contains lateral\n1216: references.  In such cases, *all* paths for that relation will be\n1217: parameterized by at least the set of relations used in its lateral\n1218: references.  (And in turn, join relations including such a subquery might\n1219: not have any unparameterized paths.)
