All the other comments made above for\n1220: parameterized paths still apply, though; in particular, each such path is\n1221: still expected to enforce any join clauses that can be pushed down to it,\n1222: so that all paths of the same parameterization have the same rowcount.\n1223: \n1224: We also allow LATERAL subqueries to be flattened (pulled up into the parent\n1225: query) by the optimizer, but only when this does not introduce lateral\n1226: references into JOIN/ON quals that would refer to relations outside the\n1227: lowest outer join at/above that qual.  The semantics of such a qual would\n1228: be unclear.  Note that even with this restriction, pullup of a LATERAL\n1229: subquery can result in creating PlaceHolderVars that contain lateral\n1230: references to relations outside their syntactic scope.  We still evaluate\n1231: such PHVs at their syntactic location or lower, but the presence of such a\n1232: PHV in the quals or targetlist of a plan node requires that node to appear\n1233: on the inside of a nestloop join relative to the rel(s) supplying the\n1234: lateral reference.  (Perhaps now that that stuff works, we could relax the\n1235: pullup restriction?)\n1236: \n1237: \n1238: Security-level constraints on qual clauses\n1239: ------------------------------------------\n1240: \n1241: To support row-level security and security-barrier views efficiently,\n1242: we mark qual clauses (RestrictInfo nodes) with a \"security_level\" field.\n1243: The basic concept is that a qual with a lower security_level must be\n1244: evaluated before one with a higher security_level.  This ensures that\n1245: \"leaky\" quals that might expose sensitive data are not evaluated until\n1246: after the security barrier quals that are supposed to filter out\n1247: security-sensitive rows.  However, many qual conditions are \"leakproof\",\n1248: that is we trust the functions they use to not expose data.  To avoid\n1249: unnecessarily inefficient plans, a leakproof qual is not delayed by\n1250: security-level considerations, even if it has a higher syntactic\n1251: security_level than another qual.\n1252: \n1253: In a query that contains no use of RLS or security-barrier views, all\n1254: quals will have security_level zero, so that none of these restrictions\n1255: kick in; we don't even need to check leakproofness of qual conditions.\n1256: \n1257: If there are security-barrier quals, they get security_level zero (and\n1258: possibly higher, if there are multiple layers of barriers).  Regular quals\n1259: coming from the query text get a security_level one more than the highest\n1260: level used for barrier quals.\n1261: \n1262: When new qual clauses are generated by EquivalenceClass processing,\n1263: they must be assigned a security_level.  This is trickier than it seems.\n1264: One's first instinct is that it would be safe to use the largest level\n1265: found among the source quals for the EquivalenceClass, but that isn't\n1266: safe at all, because it allows unwanted delays of security-barrier quals.\n1267: Consider a barrier qual \"t.x = t.y\" plus a query qual \"t.x = constant\",\n1268: and suppose there is another query qual \"leaky_function(t.z)\" that\n1269: we mustn't evaluate before the barrier qual has been checked.\n1270: We will have an EC {t.x, t.y, constant} which will lead us to replace\n1271: the EC quals with \"t.x = constant AND t.y = constant\".  (We do not want\n1272: to give up that behavior, either, since the latter condition could allow\n1273: use of an index on t.y, which we would never discover from the original\n1274: quals.)  If these generated quals are assigned the same security_level as\n1275: the query quals, then it's possible for the leaky_function qual to be\n1276: evaluated first, allowing leaky_function to see data from rows that\n1277: possibly don't pass the barrier condition.\n1278: \n1279: Instead, our handling of security levels with ECs works like this:\n1280: * Quals are not accepted as source clauses for ECs in the first place\n1281: unless they are leakproof or have security_level zero.\n1282: * EC-derived quals are assigned the minimum (not maximum) security_level\n1283: found among the EC's source clauses.\n1284: * If the maximum security_level found among the EC's source clauses is\n1285: above zero, then the equality operators selected for derived quals must\n1286: be leakproof.  When no such operator can be found, the EC is treated as\n1287: \"broken\" and we fall back to emitting its source clauses without any\n1288: additional derived quals.\n1289: \n1290: These rules together ensure that an untrusted qual clause (one with\n1291: security_level above zero) cannot cause an EC to generate a leaky derived\n1292: clause.  This makes it safe to use the minimum not maximum security_level\n1293: for derived clauses.  The rules could result in poor plans due to not\n1294: being able to generate derived clauses at all, but the risk of that is\n1295: small in practice because most btree equality operators are leakproof.\n1296: Also, by making exceptions for level-zero quals, we ensure that there is\n1297: no plan degradation when no barrier quals are present.\n1298: \n1299: Once we have security levels assigned to all clauses, enforcement\n1300: of barrier-qual ordering restrictions boils down to two rules:\n1301: \n1302: * Table scan plan nodes must not select quals for early execution\n1303: (for example, use them as index qualifiers in an indexscan) unless\n1304: they are leakproof or have security_level no higher than any other\n1305: qual that is due to be executed at the same plan node.  (Use the\n1306: utility function restriction_is_securely_promotable() to check\n1307: whether it's okay to select a qual for early execution.)\n1308: \n1309: * Normal execution of a list of quals must execute them in an order\n1310: that satisfies the same security rule, ie higher security_levels must\n1311: be evaluated later unless leakproof.  (This is handled in a single place\n1312: by order_qual_clauses() in createplan.c.)\n1313: \n1314: order_qual_clauses() uses a heuristic to decide exactly what to do with\n1315: leakproof clauses.  Normally it sorts clauses by security_level then cost,\n1316: being careful that the sort is stable so that we don't reorder clauses\n1317: without a clear reason.  But this could result in a very expensive qual\n1318: being done before a cheaper one that is of higher security_level.\n1319: If the cheaper qual is leaky we have no choice, but if it is leakproof\n1320: we could put it first.  We choose to sort leakproof quals as if they\n1321: have security_level zero, but only when their cost is less than 10X\n1322: cpu_operator_cost; that restriction alleviates the opposite problem of\n1323: doing expensive quals first just because they're leakproof.\n1324: \n1325: Additional rules will be needed to support safe handling of join quals\n1326: when there is a mix of security levels among join quals; for example, it\n1327: will be necessary to prevent leaky higher-security-level quals from being\n1328: evaluated at a lower join level than other quals of lower security level.\n1329: Currently there is no need to consider that since security-prioritized\n1330: quals can only be single-table restriction quals coming from RLS policies\n1331: or security-barrier views, and security-barrier view subqueries are never\n1332: flattened into the parent query.  Hence enforcement of security-prioritized\n1333: quals only happens at the table scan level.  With extra rules for safe\n1334: handling of security levels among join quals, it should be possible to let\n1335: security-barrier views be flattened into the parent query, allowing more\n1336: flexibility of planning while still preserving required ordering of qual\n1337: evaluation.  But that will come later.\n1338: \n1339: \n1340: Post scan/join planning\n1341: -----------------------\n1342: \n1343: So far we have discussed only scan/join planning, that is, implementation\n1344: of the FROM and WHERE clauses of a SQL query.  But the planner must also\n1345: determine how to deal with GROUP BY, aggregation, and other higher-level\n1346: features of queries; and in many cases there are multiple ways to do these\n1347: steps and thus opportunities for optimization choices.  These steps, like\n1348: scan/join planning, are handled by constructing Paths representing the\n1349: different ways to do a step, then choosing the cheapest Path.\n1350: \n1351: Since all Paths require a RelOptInfo as \"parent\", we create RelOptInfos\n1352: representing the outputs of these upper-level processing steps.  These\n1353: RelOptInfos are mostly dummy, but their pathlist lists hold all the Paths\n1354: considered useful for each step.  Currently, we may create these types of\n1355: additional RelOptInfos during upper-level planning:\n1356: \n1357: UPPERREL_SETOP\t\tresult of UNION/INTERSECT/EXCEPT, if any\n1358: UPPERREL_PARTIAL_GROUP_AGG\tresult of partial grouping/aggregation, if any\n1359: UPPERREL_GROUP_AGG\tresult of grouping/aggregation, if any\n1360: UPPERREL_WINDOW\t\tresult of window functions, if any\n1361: UPPERREL_PARTIAL_DISTINCT\tresult of partial \"SELECT DISTINCT\", if any\n1362: UPPERREL_DISTINCT\tresult of \"SELECT DISTINCT\", if any\n1363: UPPERREL_ORDERED\tresult of ORDER BY, if any\n1364: UPPERREL_FINAL\t\tresult of any remaining top-level actions\n1365: \n1366: UPPERREL_FINAL is used to represent any final processing steps, currently\n1367: LockRows (SELECT FOR UPDATE), LIMIT/OFFSET, and ModifyTable.  There is no\n1368: flexibility about the order in which these steps are done, and thus no need\n1369: to subdivide this stage more finely.\n1370: \n1371: These \"upper relations\" are identified by the UPPERREL enum values shown\n1372: above, plus a relids set, which allows there to be more than one upperrel\n1373: of the same kind.  We use NULL for the relids if there's no need for more\n1374: than one upperrel of the same kind.  Currently, in fact, the relids set\n1375: is vestigial because it's always NULL, but that's expected to change in\n1376: the future.  For example, in planning set operations, we might need the\n1377: relids to denote which subset of the leaf SELECTs has been combined in a\n1378: particular group of Paths that are competing with each other.\n1379: \n1380: The result of subquery_planner() is always returned as a set of Paths\n1381: stored in the UPPERREL_FINAL rel with NULL relids.  The other types of\n1382: upperrels are created only if needed for the particular query.\n1383: \n1384: \n1385: Parallel Query and Partial Paths\n1386: --------------------------------\n1387: \n1388: Parallel query involves dividing up the work that needs to be performed\n1389: either by an entire query or some portion of the query in such a way that\n1390: some of that work can be done by one or more worker processes, which are\n1391: called parallel workers.  Parallel workers are a subtype of dynamic\n1392: background workers; see src/backend/access/transam/README.parallel for a\n1393: fuller description.  The academic literature on parallel query suggests\n1394: that parallel execution strategies can be divided into essentially two\n1395: categories: pipelined parallelism, where the execution of the query is\n1396: divided into multiple stages and each stage is handled by a separate\n1397: process; and partitioning parallelism, where the data is split between\n1398: multiple processes and each process handles a subset of it.  The\n1399: literature, however, suggests that gains from pipeline parallelism are\n1400: often very limited due to the difficulty of avoiding pipeline stalls.\n1401: Consequently, we do not currently attempt to generate query plans that\n1402: use this technique.\n1403: \n1404: Instead, we focus on partitioning parallelism, which does not require\n1405: that the underlying table be partitioned.  It only requires that (1)\n1406: there is some method of dividing the data from at least one of the base\n1407: tables involved in the relation across multiple processes, (2) allowing\n1408: each process to handle its own portion of the data, and then (3)\n1409: collecting the results.  Requirements (2) and (3) are satisfied by the\n1410: executor node Gather (or GatherMerge), which launches any number of worker\n1411: processes and executes its single child plan in all of them, and perhaps\n1412: in the leader also, if the children aren't generating enough data to keep\n1413: the leader busy.  Requirement (1) is handled by the table scan node: when\n1414: invoked with parallel_aware = true, this node will, in effect, partition\n1415: the table on a block by block basis, returning a subset of the tuples from\n1416: the relation in each worker where that scan node is executed.\n1417: \n1418: Just as we do for non-parallel access methods, we build Paths to\n1419: represent access strategies that can be used in a parallel plan.  These\n1420: are, in essence, the same strategies that are available in the\n1421: non-parallel plan, but there is an important difference: a path that\n1422: will run beneath a Gather node returns only a subset of the query\n1423: results in each worker, not all of them.  To form a path that can\n1424: actually be executed, the (rather large) cost of the Gather node must be\n1425: accounted for.  For this reason among others, paths intended to run\n1426: beneath a Gather node - which we call \"partial\" paths since they return\n1427: only a subset of the results in each worker - must be kept separate from\n1428: ordinary paths (see RelOptInfo's partial_pathlist and the function\n1429: add_partial_path).\n1430: \n1431: One of the keys to making parallel query effective is to run as much of\n1432: the query in parallel as possible.  Therefore, we expect it to generally\n1433: be desirable to postpone the Gather stage until as near to the top of the\n1434: plan as possible.  Expanding the range of cases in which more work can be\n1435: pushed below the Gather (and costing them accurately) is likely to keep us\n1436: busy for a long time to come.\n1437: \n1438: Partitionwise joins\n1439: -------------------\n1440: \n1441: A join between two similarly partitioned tables can be broken down into joins\n1442: between their matching partitions if there exists an equi-join condition\n1443: between the partition keys of the joining tables. The equi-join between\n1444: partition keys implies that all join partners for a given row in one\n1445: partitioned table must be in the corresponding partition of the other\n1446: partitioned table. Because of this the join between partitioned tables to be\n1447: broken into joins between the matching partitions. The resultant join is\n1448: partitioned in the same way as the joining relations, thus allowing an N-way\n1449: join between similarly partitioned tables having equi-join condition between\n1450: their partition keys to be broken down into N-way joins between their matching\n1451: partitions. This technique of breaking down a join between partitioned tables\n1452: into joins between their partitions is called partitionwise join. We will use\n1453: term \"partitioned relation\" for either a partitioned table or a join between\n1454: compatibly partitioned tables.\n1455: \n1456: Even if the joining relations don't have exactly the same partition bounds,\n1457: partitionwise join can still be applied by using an advanced\n1458: partition-matching algorithm.  For both the joining relations, the algorithm\n1459: checks whether every partition of one joining relation only matches one\n1460: partition of the other joining relation at most.  In such a case the join\n1461: between the joining relations can be broken down into joins between the\n1462: matching partitions.  The join relation can then be considered partitioned.\n1463: The algorithm produces the pairs of the matching partitions, plus the\n1464: partition bounds for the join relation, to allow partitionwise join for\n1465: computing the join.  The algorithm is implemented in partition_bounds_merge().\n1466: For an N-way join relation considered partitioned this way, not every pair of\n1467: joining relations can use partitionwise join.  For example:\n1468: \n1469: \t(A leftjoin B on (Pab)) innerjoin C on (Pac)\n1470: \n1471: where A, B, and C are partitioned tables, and A has an extra partition\n1472: compared to B and C.  When considering partitionwise join for the join {A B},\n1473: the extra partition of A doesn't have a matching partition on the nullable\n1474: side, which is the case that the current implementation of partitionwise join\n1475: can't handle.  So {A B} is not considered partitioned, and the pair of {A B}\n1476: and C considered for the 3-way join can't use partitionwise join.  On the\n1477: other hand, the pair of {A C} and B can use partitionwise join because {A C}\n1478: is considered partitioned by eliminating the extra partition (see identity 1\n1479: on outer join reordering).  Whether an N-way join can use partitionwise join\n1480: is determined based on the first pair of joining relations that are both\n1481: partitioned and can use partitionwise join.\n1482: \n1483: The partitioning properties of a partitioned relation are stored in its\n1484: RelOptInfo.  The information about data types of partition keys are stored in\n1485: PartitionSchemeData structure. The planner maintains a list of canonical\n1486: partition schemes (distinct PartitionSchemeData objects) so that RelOptInfo of\n1487: any two partitioned relations with same partitioning scheme point to the same\n1488: PartitionSchemeData object.  This reduces memory consumed by\n1489: PartitionSchemeData objects and makes it easy to compare the partition schemes\n1490: of joining relations.\n1491: \n1492: Partitionwise aggregates/grouping\n1493: ---------------------------------\n1494: \n1495: If the GROUP BY clause contains all of the partition keys, all the rows\n1496: that belong to a given group must come from a single partition; therefore,\n1497: aggregation can be done completely separately for each partition. Otherwise,\n1498: partial aggregates can be computed for each partition, and then finalized\n1499: after appending the results from the individual partitions.  This technique of\n1500: breaking down aggregation or grouping over a partitioned relation into\n1501: aggregation or grouping over its partitions is called partitionwise\n1502: aggregation.  Especially when the partition keys match the GROUP BY clause,\n1503: this can be significantly faster than the regular method.\n1504: ",

  "content": "# PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline: Architecture Analysis\n\n## Files Examined\n\n### Traffic Cop (Entry Point)\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c** \u2014 Implements `exec_simple_query()`, the main entry point for simple query execution from protocol handler. Orchestrates the complete pipeline from parsing through execution.\n\n### Parser Subsystem\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:602** \u2014 `pg_parse_query()` \u2014 Invokes the lexer/parser and returns a list of `RawStmt` nodes\n- **src/backend/parser/parser.c:41** \u2014 `raw_parser()` \u2014 Entry point to the lexer and YACC-generated parser\n- **src/backend/parser/scan.l** \u2014 Flex-based lexer that tokenizes SQL text\n- **src/backend/parser/gram.y** \u2014 YACC grammar rules for SQL syntax, generates parser AST\n\n### Semantic Analyzer\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:665** \u2014 `pg_analyze_and_rewrite_fixedparams()` \u2014 Orchestrates analysis and rewriting\n- **src/backend/parser/analyze.c:104** \u2014 `parse_analyze_fixedparams()` \u2014 Main semantic analysis entry point; calls `transformTopLevelStmt()` to convert `RawStmt` to `Query`\n- **src/backend/parser/parse_agg.c, parse_clause.c, parse_expr.c, etc.** \u2014 Semantic analysis functions for specific SQL constructs (aggregates, clauses, expressions, function calls, operators, etc.)\n\n### Query Rewriter\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:798** \u2014 `pg_rewrite_query()` \u2014 Entry point for query rewriting\n- **src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c:4565** \u2014 `QueryRewrite()` \u2014 Primary rewriter implementation; applies stored rules (views, instead-of triggers) to transform queries\n\n### Optimizer/Planner Subsystem\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:882** \u2014 `pg_plan_query()` \u2014 Wraps planner invocation for a single query\n- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:970** \u2014 `pg_plan_queries()` \u2014 Plans a list of `Query` nodes; returns list of `PlannedStmt` nodes\n- **src/backend/optimizer/plan/planner.c:287** \u2014 `planner()` \u2014 Hook-aware entry point to the planner\n- **src/backend/optimizer/plan/planner.c:303** \u2014 `standard_planner()` \u2014 Core planner implementation; calls `subquery_planner()` for path generation, then selects best path via `get_cheapest_fractional_path()` and calls `create_plan()`\n- **src/backend/optimizer/plan/planmain.c** \u2014 `subquery_planner()` \u2014 Plans a single SELECT, invokes path generation and upper relation planning via `grouping_planner()`\n- **src/backend/optimizer/path/allpaths.c** \u2014 Path generation module; builds all possible access paths via `make_rel_from_joinlist()`, `make_join_rel()`, adds scan/join paths\n- **src/backend/optimizer/plan/createplan.c** \u2014 `create_plan()` \u2014 Converts best `Path` to executable `Plan` tree\n\n### Executor Subsystem\n- **src/backend/tcop/pquery.c:433** \u2014 `PortalStart()` \u2014 Initializes portal for execution; calls `ExecutorStart()`\n- **src/backend/executor/execMain.c:121** \u2014 `ExecutorStart()` \u2014 Initializes executor state; calls `ExecInitNode()` to build `PlanState` tree\n- **src/backend/tcop/pquery.c** \u2014 `PortalRun()` \u2014 Executes portal; drives tuple fetch loop by repeatedly calling `ExecutorRun()`\n- **src/backend/executor/execMain.c** \u2014 `ExecutorRun()` \u2014 Main execution loop; calls `ExecutePlan()` which repeatedly invokes `ExecProcNode()` on plan root\n- **src/backend/executor/execProcnode.c** \u2014 Volcano-style executor dispatch:\n  - `ExecInitNode()` \u2014 Recursively initializes plan tree into `PlanState` tree (line 141)\n  - `ExecProcNode()` \u2014 Dispatches node execution via function pointers; each node calls `ExecProcNode()` on children for pull-driven evaluation\n  - `ExecEndNode()` \u2014 Recursively finalizes nodes\n- **src/backend/executor/nodeSeqscan.c, nodeIndexscan.c, nodeHashjoin.c, nodeNestloop.c, etc.** \u2014 Individual executor node implementations; each implements `ExecInit*()`, `Exec*()`, and `ExecEnd*()` functions\n\n### Node Type Definitions\n- **src/include/nodes/parsenodes.h:2081** \u2014 `RawStmt` structure; output of parser, contains unparsed statement text + AST node\n- **src/include/nodes/parsenodes.h:117** \u2014 `Query` structure; output of semantic analyzer; normalized query representation with SELECT/INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE-specific fields\n- **src/include/nodes/plannodes.h:46** \u2014 `PlannedStmt` structure; output of planner; contains final executable plan tree + metadata\n- **src/include/nodes/plannodes.h:158** \u2014 `Plan` structure; base class for all plan node types\n- **src/include/nodes/pathnodes.h** \u2014 `Path` structure hierarchy; used during optimization phase to represent candidate execution paths (SeqScan, IndexScan, HashJoin, etc.)\n- **src/include/nodes/execnodes.h** \u2014 `PlanState` and related execution state structures; runtime state for each plan node instance\n\n---\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n### 1. Entry Point\n```\nexec_simple_query() [postgres.c:1011]\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500 Reports query for monitoring (pgstat_report_activity)\n\u251c\u2500 Starts transaction (start_xact_command)\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\n\u2502  STAGE 1: PARSING\n\u251c\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\u2500\n\u2502\n\u2514\u2500> pg_parse_query(query_string) [postgres.c:603]\n    \u2502\n    \u2514\u2500> raw_parser(query_string, RAW_PARSE_DEFAULT) [parser.c:42]\n        \u2502\n        \u251c\u2500 Lexical analysis (scan.l) \u2192 Tokens\n        \u2502\n        \u2514\u2500 Syntactic analysis (gram.y) \u2192 RawStmt AST\n            Returns: List<RawStmt>\n\n    Output: List of RawStmt nodes\n    (Each contains unparsed statement text + AST root)\n```\n\n### 2. Portal Definition & Execution\n```\nexec_simple_query() continuation:\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> CreatePortal(\"\", true, true) [portal creation]\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> PortalDefineQuery(portal, NULL, pointer\n\u2502               \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Dispatch to specific node executor\n\u2502               \u2502   \u2502   \u251c\u2500 ExecSeqScan() \u2192 Fetches tuple from heap, applies        \u2502   \u2502   \u251c\u2500 etc. (all 60+ executor node types)\n\u2502               \u2502   \u2502\n\u2502               \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Returns TupleTableSlot (wraps tuple data)\n\u2502               \u2502\n\u2502               \u251c\u2500 Process tuple (apply projections, qualifications if needed)\n\u2502               \u251c\u2500 Send to receiver (network, file, etc.)\n\u2502               \u2502\n\u2502               \u2514\u2500 Repeat until no more tuples\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> ExecutorFinish(QueryDesc *queryDesc) [execMain.c]\n\u2502   \u2514\u2500 Flush any pending output (aggregates, etc.)\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> ExecutorEnd(QueryDesc *queryDesc) [execMain.c]\n\u2502   \u2502\n\u2502   \u2514\u2500> ExecEndNode(PlanState *node) [execProcnode.c]\n\u2502       \u2502\n\u2502       \u251c\u2500 Recursively finalize all nodes\n\u2502       \u251c\u2500 Close relations, indexes, temporary files\n\u2502       \u251c\u2500 Free memory\n\u2502       \u2502\n\u2502       \u2514\u2500 Dispatch to specific node finalizers (ExecEndSeqScan, etc.)\n\u2502\n\u2514\u2500 PortalDrop(portal, false)\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### 1. Query Parsing Pipeline\nThe **parser stage** transforms unstructured SQL text into a structured Abstract Syntax Tree (RawStmt). It employs a two-phase approach:\n- **Lexical Analysis (scan.l)**: A Flex-generated lexer tokenizes the input stream, identifying keywords, identifiers, operators, and literals.\n- **Syntactic Analysis (gram.y)**: A YACC-generated bottom-up parser applies grammar rules to construct a tree of statement nodes. The parser is generic and produces AST nodes without semantic interpretation (no name resolution, type checking, or validation).\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/parser/parser.c` \u2014 wrapper function\n- `src/backend/parser/scan.l` \u2014 lexer\n- `src/backend/parser/gram.y` \u2014 grammar rules\n- Output: `RawStmt` (contains parsed statement node tree)\n\n### 2. Semantic Analysis Phase\nThe **analyzer stage** transforms the syntactic AST into a logical query tree (Query). It performs:\n- **Name Resolution**: Maps table aliases and column references to actual catalog objects (relations, attributes).\n- **Type Inference & Coercion**: Determines expression result types, inserts type-casting operations where needed.\n- **Function Lookup & Validation**: Resolves function calls to actual functions, validates argument types.\n- **Aggregate Processing**: Groups aggregate functions, ensures they appear only in appropriate contexts.\n- **Subquery Handling**: Flattens or defers subquery processing based on context.\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/parser/analyze.c` \u2014 main entry point (`parse_analyze_fixedparams`, `transformTopLevelStmt`)\n- `src/backend/parser/parse_*.c` \u2014 semantic rules for specific constructs\n- Output: `Query` (normalized, semantically validated representation)\n\n### 3. Query Rewriting System\nThe **rewriter stage** applies user-defined rules and view definitions to transform queries before planning. It:\n- **Expands Views**: Replaces view references in FROM clause with the view's definition.\n- **Applies Rules**: Substitutes rules defined via `CREATE RULE`, enabling powerful query transformations (e.g., security policies, materialized view maintenance).\n- **Applies Triggers**: Processes `INSTEAD OF` triggers on views.\n- **May expand to multiple queries**: A single query can be rewritten into multiple queries (e.g., cascading deletes via rules).\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c` \u2014 `QueryRewrite()` function\n- Output: `List<Query>` (potentially expanded)\n\n### 4. Two-Phase Optimization (Path Generation & Plan Creation)\n\nThe **optimizer stage** is split into two distinct phases:\n\n#### Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)\nThe path enumeration phase generates multiple candidate execution paths, each representing a different way to execute the query. For each relation in the FROM clause, it generates:\n- **Base scan paths**: SeqScan, IndexScan, BitmapIndexScan (for single-relation access patterns)\n- **Multi-relation paths**: Join paths combining different relations (NestedLoop, HashJoin, MergeJoin)\n- **Upper-level paths**: For GROUP BY, ORDER BY, aggregates, limits\n\nEach path has a cost model (estimated CPU and I/O cost). The system explores join orders (via dynamic programming in `make_rel_from_joinlist`) and join methods.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/optimizer/path/allpaths.c` \u2014 path generation functions\n- `src/backend/optimizer/path/*.c` \u2014 join algorithm implementations (costsize.c estimates costs)\n- Output: `RelOptInfo` with list of `Path` candidates (each with estimated cost)\n\n#### Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)\nThe plan generation phase converts the best `Path` (selected via `get_cheapest_fractional_path`) into an executable `Plan` tree. This involves:\n- **Recursive conversion**: Each `Path` is converted to a corresponding `Plan` node type.\n- **Compilation of expressions**: Filter conditions and projections are compiled into executable form.\n- **Memory management**: Decisions about buffer/materialization strategies.\n- **Parallel operator setup**: If applicable, inserts Gather/GatherMerge nodes for parallel execution.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/optimizer/plan/createplan.c` \u2014 `create_plan()` function\n- Output: `PlannedStmt` (containing root `Plan` node + metadata)\n\n### 5. Portal-Based Execution Framework\nExecution is **portal-based**, providing abstraction for multiple execution models:\n- **Simple execution**: Execute to completion.\n- **Cursor-like execution**: Fetch tuples incrementally via `FETCH` commands.\n- **Prepared statement caching**: Reuse plans across multiple executions.\n\nThe portal holds the plan, stores execution state between fetches, and manages result set cursors.\n\n**Key functions**:\n- `PortalStart()` \u2014 Initializes portal execution; calls `ExecutorStart()`\n- `PortalRun()` \u2014 Drives execution loop; calls `ExecutorRun()` repeatedly\n- Output: Tuples sent to receiver (network, file, output format specified)\n\n### 6. Volcano-Style Executor Architecture\nThe **executor** implements a **pull-based, tuple-at-a-time model** (Volcano/Cascades architecture):\n\n**Three-phase execution per plan node**:\n1. **Initialization Phase** (`ExecInitNode`)\n   - Allocates node-specific state (e.g., hash table for joins, sort state for sorts).\n   - Recursively initializes child plan nodes.\n   - Opens base relations and indexes as needed.\n   - Returns a `PlanState` tree mirroring the `Plan` tree.\n\n2. **Execution Phase** (`ExecProcNode` \u2192 node-specific `Exec*` function)\n   - Called repeatedly by parent node to fetch next tuple.\n   - **Pull-based semantics**: Child nodes called to fetch input, not pushed by parents.\n   - Returns a `TupleTableSlot` wrapping the next result tuple (or NULL if exhausted).\n   - **Node-specific logic**:\n     - **Scan nodes** (SeqScan, IndexScan): Fetch from storage layer, apply filters.\n     - **Join nodes** (HashJoin, NestLoop, MergeJoin): Combine tuples from left/right children.\n     - **Aggregate nodes** (Agg, GroupAggregate): Accumulate state, emit group results.\n     - **Sort nodes** (Sort, IncrementalSort): Buffer input, sort in-memory or on disk, return sorted stream.\n     - **Limit nodes** (Limit): Count output tuples, stop after N.\n\n3. **Finalization Phase** (`ExecEndNode`)\n   - Cleans up node-specific state.\n   - Closes relations and indexes.\n   - Frees memory.\n   - Recursively finalizes child nodes.\n\n**Dispatcher mechanism** (execProcnode.c):\n- Each `PlanState` holds a function pointer to its executor function.\n- `ExecProcNode()` calls this pointer, dispatching to the correct implementation.\n- Enables polymorphic node execution without explicit type checks.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/executor/execProcnode.c` \u2014 Dispatcher framework and state initialization\n- `src/backend/executor/execMain.c` \u2014 Main ExecutorStart/Run/Finish/End loop\n- `src/backend/executor/node*.c` \u2014 60+ individual node implementations\n\n### 7. Data Structures & Type Hierarchy\n\n**Core node types used in the pipeline**:\n- **RawStmt** (parsenodes.h): Parser output; minimal semantics, unvalidated names/types.\n- **Query** (parsenodes.h): Analyzer output; full semantics, catalog-resolved names, type-checked expressions.\n- **RelOptInfo / Path** (pathnodes.h): Optimizer internal; represents candidate access plans with cost estimates.\n- **Plan / PlanState** (plannodes.h / execnodes.h): Planner output; executable plan tree and runtime state.\n- **TupleTableSlot** (execnodes.h): Executor runtime; holds tuple data and attribute information during execution.\n\n**Node type dispatch**:\n- `NodeTag` enum field in every node allows polymorphic dispatching.\n- `ExecInitNode`, `ExecProcNode`, `ExecEndNode` use switch statements on `NodeTag` to call appropriate handlers.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline follows a **traditional compiler architecture**:\n1. **Parser** (scan.l + gram.y) \u2192 `RawStmt` (syntactic AST)\n2. **Semantic Analyzer** (analyze.c) \u2192 `Query` (normalized logical tree)\n3. **Rewriter** (rewriteHandler.c) \u2192 `Query` (rule-rewritten)\n4. **Optimizer** (planner.c + allpaths.c + createplan.c) \u2192 `PlannedStmt` (executable plan)\n5. **Executor** (execMain.c + execProcnode.c + node*.c) \u2192 tuple stream (results)\n\nThe optimizer uses a **two-phase approach**: Path enumeration via dynamic programming (exploring join orders and methods) followed by cost-based selection and plan compilation. The executor implements the **Volcano tuple-at-a-time pull-based model**, where each plan node processes input from children on-demand, enabling efficient streaming and late materialization. This architecture provides extensibility (custom nodes, FDWs, parallel execution) while maintaining predictable execution semantics.\n",

               \u2502\n                \u251c\u2500> get_cheapest_fractional_path(final_rel, tuple_fraction) [pathnode.c]\n                \u2502   \u2192 Selects best Path (lowest cost for given \u251c\u2500> create_nestloop_plan() \u2192 NestLoop plan node\n                    \u2502   \u251c\u2500> create_sort_plan() \u2192 Sort plan node\n                    \u2502   \u251c\u2500> (executable representation)\n\n                Returns: PlannedStmt (contains plan tree + metadata)\n\n    Output: List of PlannedStmt nodes\n```\n\n### 4. Portal Definition & Execution\n```\nexec_simple_query() continuation:\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> CreatePortal(\"\", true, true) [portal creation]\n\u2502\n\u251c\u2500> PortalDefineQuery(portal, NULL, memory\n\u2502       \u2502\n\u2502       \u2514\u2500 Dispatch to specific node finalizers (ExecEndSeqScan, etc.)\n\u2502\n\u2514\u2500 PortalDrop(portal, false)\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### 1. Query Parsing Pipeline\nThe **parser stage** transforms unstructured SQL text into a structured Abstract Syntax Tree (RawStmt). It employs a two-phase approach:\n- **Lexical Analysis (scan.l)**: A Flex-generated lexer tokenizes the input stream, identifying keywords, identifiers, operators, and literals.\n- **Syntactic Analysis (gram.y)**: A YACC-generated bottom-up parser applies grammar rules to construct a tree of statement nodes. The parser is generic and produces AST nodes without semantic interpretation (no name resolution, type checking, or validation).\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/parser/parser.c` \u2014 wrapper function\n- `src/backend/parser/scan.l` \u2014 lexer\n- `src/backend/parser/gram.y` \u2014 grammar rules\n- Output: `RawStmt` (contains parsed statement node tree)\n\n### 2. Semantic Analysis Phase\nThe **analyzer stage** transforms the syntactic AST into a logical query tree (Query). It performs:\n- **Name Resolution**: Maps table aliases and column references to actual catalog objects (relations, attributes).\n- **Type Inference & Coercion**: Determines expression result types, inserts type-casting operations where needed.\n- **Function Lookup & Validation**: Resolves function calls to actual functions, validates argument types.\n- **Aggregate Processing**: Groups aggregate functions, ensures they appear only in appropriate contexts.\n- **Subquery Handling**: Flattens or defers subquery processing based on context.\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/parser/analyze.c` \u2014 main entry point (`parse_analyze_fixedparams`, `transformTopLevelStmt`)\n- `src/backend/parser/parse_*.c` \u2014 semantic rules for specific constructs\n- Output: `Query` (normalized, semantically validated representation)\n\n### 3. Query Rewriting System\nThe **rewriter stage** applies user-defined rules and view definitions to transform queries before planning. It:\n- **Expands Views**: Replaces view references in FROM clause with the view's definition.\n- **Applies Rules**: Substitutes rules defined via `CREATE RULE`, enabling powerful query transformations (e.g., security policies, materialized view maintenance).\n- **Applies Triggers**: Processes `INSTEAD OF` triggers on views.\n- **May expand to multiple queries**: A single query can be rewritten into multiple queries (e.g., cascading deletes via rules).\n\n**Key files involved**:\n- `src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c` \u2014 `QueryRewrite()` function\n- Output: `List<Query>` (potentially expanded)\n\n### 4. Two-Phase Optimization (Path Generation & Plan Creation)\n\nThe **optimizer stage** is split into two distinct phases:\n\n#### Phase 1: Path Generation (allpaths.c)\nThe path enumeration phase generates multiple candidate execution paths, each representing a different way to execute the query. For each relation in the FROM clause, it generates:\n- **Base scan paths**: SeqScan, IndexScan, BitmapIndexScan (for single-relation access patterns)\n- **Multi-relation paths**: Join paths combining different relations (NestedLoop, HashJoin, MergeJoin)\n- **Upper-level paths**: For GROUP BY, ORDER BY, aggregates, limits\n\nEach path has a cost model (estimated CPU and I/O cost). The system explores join orders (via dynamic programming in `make_rel_from_joinlist`) and join methods.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/optimizer/path/allpaths.c` \u2014 path generation functions\n- `src/backend/optimizer/path/*.c` \u2014 join algorithm implementations (costsize.c estimates costs)\n- Output: `RelOptInfo` with list of `Path` candidates (each with estimated cost)\n\n#### Phase 2: Plan Creation (createplan.c)\nThe plan generation phase converts the best `Path` (selected via `get_cheapest_fractional_path`) into an executable `Plan` tree. This involves:\n- **Recursive conversion**: Each `Path` is converted to a corresponding `Plan` node type.\n- **Compilation of expressions**: Filter conditions and projections are compiled into executable form.\n- **Memory management**: Decisions about buffer/materialization strategies.\n- **Parallel operator setup**: If applicable, inserts Gather/GatherMerge nodes for parallel execution.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/optimizer/plan/createplan.c` \u2014 `create_plan()` function\n- Output: `PlannedStmt` (containing root `Plan` node + metadata)\n\n### 5. Portal-Based Execution Framework\nExecution is **portal-based**, providing abstraction for multiple execution models:\n- **Simple execution**: Execute to completion.\n- **Cursor-like execution**: Fetch tuples incrementally via `FETCH` commands.\n- **Prepared statement caching**: Reuse plans across multiple executions.\n\nThe portal holds the plan, stores execution state between fetches, and manages result set cursors.\n\n**Key functions**:\n- `PortalStart()` \u2014 Initializes portal execution; calls `ExecutorStart()`\n- `PortalRun()` \u2014 Drives execution loop; calls `ExecutorRun()` repeatedly\n- Output: Tuples sent to receiver (network, file, output format specified)\n\n### 6. Volcano-Style Executor Architecture\nThe **executor** implements a **pull-based, tuple-at-a-time model** (Volcano/Cascades architecture):\n\n**Three-phase execution per plan node**:\n1. **Initialization Phase** (`ExecInitNode`)\n   - Allocates node-specific state (e.g., hash table for joins, sort state for sorts).\n   - Recursively initializes child plan nodes.\n   - Opens base relations and indexes as needed.\n   - Returns a `PlanState` tree mirroring the `Plan` tree.\n\n2. **Execution Phase** (`ExecProcNode` \u2192 node-specific `Exec*` function)\n   - Called repeatedly by parent node to fetch next tuple.\n   - **Pull-based semantics**: Child nodes called to fetch input, not pushed by parents.\n   - Returns a `TupleTableSlot` wrapping the next result tuple (or NULL if exhausted).\n   - **Node-specific logic**:\n     - **Scan nodes** (SeqScan, IndexScan): Fetch from storage layer, apply filters.\n     - **Join nodes** (HashJoin, NestLoop, MergeJoin): Combine tuples from left/right children.\n     - **Aggregate nodes** (Agg, GroupAggregate): Accumulate state, emit group results.\n     - **Sort nodes** (Sort, IncrementalSort): Buffer input, sort in-memory or on disk, return sorted stream.\n     - **Limit nodes** (Limit): Count output tuples, stop after N.\n\n3. **Finalization Phase** (`ExecEndNode`)\n   - Cleans up node-specific state.\n   - Closes relations and indexes.\n   - Frees memory.\n   - Recursively finalizes child nodes.\n\n**Dispatcher mechanism** (execProcnode.c):\n- Each `PlanState` holds a function pointer to its executor function.\n- `ExecProcNode()` calls this pointer, dispatching to the correct implementation.\n- Enables polymorphic node execution without explicit type checks.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/executor/execProcnode.c` \u2014 Dispatcher framework and state initialization\n- `src/backend/executor/execMain.c` \u2014 Main ExecutorStart/Run/Finish/End loop\n- `src/backend/executor/node*.c` \u2014 60+ individual node implementations\n\n### 7. Data Structures & Type Hierarchy\n\n**Core node types used in the pipeline**:\n- **RawStmt** (parsenodes.h): Parser output; minimal semantics, unvalidated names/types.\n- **Query** (parsenodes.h): Analyzer output; full semantics, catalog-resolved names, type-checked expressions.\n- **RelOptInfo / Path** (pathnodes.h): Optimizer internal; represents candidate access plans with cost estimates.\n- **Plan / PlanState** (plannodes.h / execnodes.h): Planner output; executable plan tree and runtime state.\n- **TupleTableSlot** (execnodes.h): Executor runtime; holds tuple data and attribute information during execution.\n\n**Node type dispatch**:\n- `NodeTag` enum field in every node allows polymorphic dispatching.\n- `ExecInitNode`, `ExecProcNode`, `ExecEndNode` use switch statements on `NodeTag` to call appropriate handlers.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline follows a **traditional compiler architecture**:\n1. **Parser** (scan.l + gram.y) \u2192 `RawStmt` (syntactic AST)\n2. **Semantic Analyzer** (analyze.c) \u2192 `Query` (normalized logical tree)\n3. **Rewriter** (rewriteHandler.c) \u2192 `Query` (rule-rewritten)\n4. **Optimizer** (planner.c + allpaths.c + createplan.c) \u2192 `PlannedStmt` (executable plan)\n5. **Executor** (execMain.c + execProcnode.c + node*.c) \u2192 tuple stream (results)\n\nThe optimizer uses a **two-phase approach**: Path enumeration via dynamic programming (exploring join orders and methods) followed by cost-based selection and plan compilation. The executor implements the **Volcano tuple-at-a-time pull-based model**, where each plan node processes input from children on-demand, enabling efficient streaming and late materialization. This architecture provides extensibility (custom nodes, FDWs, parallel execution) while maintaining predictable execution semantics.\n",

  "stdout": "435 /logs/agent/solution.md\n   - **Pull-based semantics**: Child nodes called to fetch input, not pushed by parents.\n   - Returns a `TupleTableSlot` wrapping the next result tuple (or NULL if exhausted).\n   - **Node-specific logic**:\n     - **Scan nodes** (SeqScan, IndexScan): Fetch from storage layer, apply filters.\n     - **Join nodes** (HashJoin, NestLoop, MergeJoin): Combine tuples from left/right children.\n     - **Aggregate nodes** (Agg, GroupAggregate): Accumulate state, emit group results.\n     - **Sort nodes** (Sort, IncrementalSort): Buffer input, sort in-memory or on disk, return sorted stream.\n     - **Limit nodes** (Limit): Count output tuples, stop after N.\n\n3. **Finalization Phase** (`ExecEndNode`)\n   - Cleans up node-specific state.\n   - Closes relations and indexes.\n   - Frees memory.\n   - Recursively finalizes child nodes.\n\n**Dispatcher mechanism** (execProcnode.c):\n- Each `PlanState` holds a function pointer to its executor function.\n- `ExecProcNode()` calls this pointer, dispatching to the correct implementation.\n- Enables polymorphic node execution without explicit type checks.\n\n**Key files**:\n- `src/backend/executor/execProcnode.c` \u2014 Dispatcher framework and state initialization\n- `src/backend/executor/execMain.c` \u2014 Main ExecutorStart/Run/Finish/End loop\n- `src/backend/executor/node*.c` \u2014 60+ individual node implementations\n\n### 7. Data Structures & Type Hierarchy\n\n**Core node types used in the pipeline**:\n- **RawStmt** (parsenodes.h): Parser output; minimal semantics, unvalidated names/types.\n- **Query** (parsenodes.h): Analyzer output; full semantics, catalog-resolved names, type-checked expressions.\n- **RelOptInfo / Path** (pathnodes.h): Optimizer internal; represents candidate access plans with cost estimates.\n- **Plan / PlanState** (plannodes.h / execnodes.h): Planner output; executable plan tree and runtime state.\n- **TupleTableSlot** (execnodes.h): Executor runtime; holds tuple data and attribute information during execution.\n\n**Node type dispatch**:\n- `NodeTag` enum field in every node allows polymorphic dispatching.\n- `ExecInitNode`, `ExecProcNode`, `ExecEndNode` use switch statements on `NodeTag` to call appropriate handlers.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline follows a **traditional compiler architecture**:\n1. **Parser** (scan.l + gram.y) \u2192 `RawStmt` (syntactic AST)\n2. **Semantic Analyzer** (analyze.c) \u2192 `Query` (normalized logical tree)\n3. **Rewriter** (rewriteHandler.c) \u2192 `Query` (rule-rewritten)\n4. **Optimizer** (planner.c + allpaths.c + createplan.c) \u2192 `PlannedStmt` (executable plan)\n5. **Executor** (execMain.c + execProcnode.c + node*.c) \u2192 tuple stream (results)\n\nThe optimizer uses a **two-phase approach**: Path enumeration via dynamic programming (exploring join orders and methods) followed by cost-based selection and plan compilation. The executor implements the **Volcano tuple-at-a-time pull-based model**, where each plan node processes input from children on-demand, enabling efficient streaming and late materialization. This architecture provides extensibility (custom nodes, FDWs, parallel execution) while maintaining predictable execution semantics."

# PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline: Architecture Analysis

## Files Examined

### Traffic Cop (Entry Point)
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c** — Implements `exec_simple_query()`, the main entry point for simple query execution from protocol handler. Orchestrates the complete pipeline from parsing through execution.

### Parser Subsystem
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:602** — `pg_parse_query()` — Invokes the lexer/parser and returns a list of `RawStmt` nodes
- **src/backend/parser/parser.c:41** — `raw_parser()` — Entry point to the lexer and YACC-generated parser
- **src/backend/parser/scan.l** — Flex-based lexer that tokenizes SQL text
- **src/backend/parser/gram.y** — YACC grammar rules for SQL syntax, generates parser AST

### Semantic Analyzer
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:665** — `pg_analyze_and_rewrite_fixedparams()` — Orchestrates analysis and rewriting
- **src/backend/parser/analyze.c:104** — `parse_analyze_fixedparams()` — Main semantic analysis entry point; calls `transformTopLevelStmt()` to convert `RawStmt` to `Query`
- **src/backend/parser/parse_agg.c, parse_clause.c, parse_expr.c, etc.** — Semantic analysis functions for specific SQL constructs (aggregates, clauses, expressions, function calls, operators, etc.)

### Query Rewriter
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:798** — `pg_rewrite_query()` — Entry point for query rewriting
- **src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c:4565** — `QueryRewrite()` — Primary rewriter implementation; applies stored rules (views, instead-of triggers) to transform queries

### Optimizer/Planner Subsystem
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:882** — `pg_plan_query()` — Wraps planner invocation for a single query
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:970** — `pg_plan_queries()` — Plans a list of `Query` nodes; returns list of `PlannedStmt` nod
... (truncated)
